<t0>I</t0>
<t1>Jonathan Harker's diary</t1>
<t2>(written in stenography)</t2>
<t3>May 3rd.</t3> <t4>Bistriţa2 - 8 PM on the evening of May 1st.</t4> <t5>We left Munich at 8:35 and arrived in Vienna early the next morning; we should have arrived at 6:46, but the train was an hour late.</t5> <t6>From what I could see from the train and the short walk I took in the streets, Budapest looks like a wonderful place.</t6> <t7>Since we arrived late in Budapest and would be departing as close to the scheduled time as possible, I was afraid to stray too far from the station.</t7> <t8>I got the impression that we were leaving the West and entering the East; the most Western of the magnificent bridges over the Danube River, with its noble breadth and depth, carried us into the traditions of Turkish rule.</t8>
<t9>We departed with quite good timing and arrived in Klausenburg after nightfall.</t9> <t10>I spent the night here at the Royale Hotel.</t10> <t11>For dinner, or rather a late-night dinner, I had chicken cooked with red pepper; it tasted very good but was quite thirst-inducing.</t11> <t12>(Note: Get the recipe for Mina.)</t12> <t13>I asked the waiter and he said it was called paprika hendl and that I could find it everywhere in the Carpathian Mountains as it is a local dish.</t13> <t14>My broken German was very useful here; I really don't know how I would have managed without it.</t14>
<t15>When I had some time to myself in London, I visited the British Museum and did some research in the library among books and maps related to Transylvania; I thought that having some preliminary knowledge about the country wouldn't be insignificant when negotiating with a nobleman from that country.</t15> <t16>I saw that the region it is named after is at the easternmost edge of the country, at the border of Transylvania, Moldavia, and Bukovina, in the middle of the Carpathian Mountains; one of the wildest and least known parts of Europe.</t16> <t17>I couldn't find a map or study that gave the exact location of Dracula's Castle, as this country didn't have maps comparable to our detailed Ordnance Survey maps; but I saw that the town of Bistriţa, which Count Dracula named, was a fairly well-known place.</t17> <t18>While talking to Mina about my journey, I will record some of my notes here as they might refresh my memory.</t18>
<t19>There are four different nationalities in the population of Transylvania: in the south, the Saxons and the Wallachians, who are mixed with them and descended from the Dacians; in the west, the Hungarians; in the east and north, the Székelys.</t19> <t20>I am going among the last group, who claim to be descended from Attila and the Huns.</t20> <t21>This claim might be true because when the Hungarians conquered the country in the eleventh century, they found that the Huns had already settled there.</t21> <t22>I read that all known superstitions in the world seem to gather in the horseshoe formed by the Carpathian Mountains as if it were the center of a fanciful vortex; if so, my visit could be very interesting.</t22> <t23>(Note: I must ask the Count about all this.</t23><t24>)</t24>
<t25>Although my bed was comfortable enough, I couldn't sleep well because I had all sorts of strange dreams.</t25> <t26>A dog barked under my window all night; maybe my sleeplessness had something to do with that; or maybe it was because of the red pepper since I had to drink all the water in my pitcher but still couldn't quench my thirst.</t26> <t27>I fell asleep towards morning and woke up to the persistent knocking on my door; at that moment, I think I was in a deep sleep.</t27> <t28>For breakfast, I ate some more red pepper, a type of porridge made from cornmeal which they said was called mamaliga, and a dish called impletata, which is eggplant stuffed with minced meat and is an excellent meal.</t28> <t29>(Note: Get the recipe for this as well.)</t29> <t30>Since the train was supposed to leave a little before eight - or rather it was supposed to leave at 7.</t30> <t31>30, I had to rush my breakfast because after running to the station, I had to sit in the carriage for more than an hour before we started moving.</t31> <t32>It seems to me that as we move further east, the trains get more delayed.</t32> <t33>Who knows what they are like in China?</t33>
<t34>Throughout the day, we slowly progressed through a terrain filled with all kinds of beauty.</t34> <t35>Sometimes, at the tops of steep hills, we saw small villages and castles similar to those we had seen in old prayer books; sometimes, we passed by rivers and streams with wide, stony banks that suggested they had been subjected to great floods.</t35> <t36>It takes a large amount of strong flowing water to sweep and clean the outer edges of a river.</t36> <t37>At every train station, there were crowds of people dressed in various kinds of attire, sometimes large gatherings.</t37> <t38>Some wore short jackets, round hats, and homemade trousers, just like those in our region or those I saw while passing through France and Germany; but others were very interesting.</t38> <t39>The women looked beautiful unless you got close to them, but their waistlines were quite thick.</t39> <t40>All of their clothes had white sleeves in one way or another; many wore wide belts with lots of ribbons hanging from them, resembling ballet dresses; but of course, they had underskirts underneath.</t40> <t41>The strangest people we saw were the Slovaks, who looked more barbaric than the rest with their large cowboy hats, wide, sack-like dirty white trousers, white linen shirts, and enormous, heavy belts almost a foot wide, studded all over with brass nails.</t41> <t42>They wore high boots into which they tucked their trousers, and they had long black hair and thick black mustaches.</t42> <t43>They were very impressive but not attractive.</t43> <t44>They could be placed on stage right now as an old Eastern bandit gang.</t44> <t45>But as I was told, they were quite harmless and moreover, people who lacked self-defense skills.</t45>
<t46>When we arrived in Bistriţa, a very interesting old settlement, it was the dark side of twilight.</t46> <t47>Since it is almost on the border - as the Borgo Pass starts there and extends to Bukovina - it has quite a stormy nature and definitely bears the traces of it.</t47> <t48>Fifty years ago, there was a series of major fires that caused terrible damage at five different times.</t48> <t49>At the very beginning of the seventeenth century, the city was subjected to a siege that lasted three weeks and lost 13,000 people from its population; war-related losses increased due to famine and disease.</t49>
<t50>Count Dracula directed me with great pleasure to the Golden Krone Hotel, which I saw was old-fashioned in every way; because, of course, I wanted to see all the traditions unique to the country.</t50> <t51>Clearly, I was expected, for as I approached the door, I was met by a cheerful elderly woman in traditional peasant attire - a long white undergarment, with a colorful apron made of fabrics at the front and back that seemed too tight for modesty.</t51> <t52>As I approached, she greeted me with a nod and said, "English Herr?"</t52> <t53>"Yes," I said, "Jonathan Harker." She smiled and spoke to an elderly man in a white-sleeved shirt who had followed her to the door.</t53> <t54>The man left but quickly returned with a letter in hand:</t54>
<t55>My friend,</t55>
<t56>Welcome to the Carpathian Mountains.</t56> <t57>I am eagerly awaiting you.</t57> <t58>Sleep well tonight.</t58> <t59>Tomorrow at three o'clock, the stagecoach will depart for Bukovina; a seat has been reserved for you.</t59> <t60>My carriage will wait for you at the Borgo Pass and will bring you to me.</t60> <t61>I hope your journey from London has been pleasant and that you will enjoy the time you spend in my beautiful lands.</t61>
<t62>Your friend</t62>
<t63>DRACULA</t63>
<t64>May 4th.</t64> <t65>- I learned that the hotel owner had received a letter from the Count instructing him to arrange the best seat in the carriage for me; however, when I asked questions about the details, he somehow seemed tight-lipped and acted as if he didn't understand my German.</t65> <t66>Yet this couldn't be true because until that moment he had perfectly understood my German; at the very least, he was definitely answering my questions as if he understood me.</t66> <t67>He and his wife, the elderly lady who greeted me, looked at each other with an expression full of fear.</t67> <t68>The man mumbled that the money was sent within the letter and that this was all he knew.</t68> <t69>When I asked him if he knew Count Dracula and if he could tell me anything about his castle, both he and his wife crossed themselves and refused to speak further, saying they knew nothing.</t69> <t70>The departure time was so close that I had no time to ask anyone else, for everything was very mysterious and, in any case, not at all reassuring.</t70>
<t71>Just before leaving, the elderly woman came to my room and, in an extremely distraught manner, said:</t71>
<t72>“Is it necessary for you to leave?</t72> <t73>Ah! Young Herr, is it necessary for you to leave?” He was so excited that he seemed to have lost his command of German and mixed it with another language I did not know.</t73> <t74>I could only understand him by asking a lot of questions.</t74> <t75>When I told him that I needed to leave as soon as possible and that I was busy with an important task, he asked once more:</t75>
<t76>"Do you know what today is?"</t76> <t77>I replied that it was the fourth of May.</t77> <t78>She shook her head from side to side and spoke again:</t78>
<t79>“Ah, yes! I know him, I know him!</t79> <t80>But do you know what today is?”</t80> <t81>When I said I didn't understand, he continued:</t81>
<t82>“Today is the eve of St. George's Day.</t82> <t83>Don't you know that when the clock strikes midnight tonight, all the evil forces in the world will be free to roam around?</t83> <t84>Do you know where you are going, what you are going towards?”</t84> <t85>He was in such a state of obvious anxiety that I tried to comfort him, but it had no effect.</t85> <t86>Finally, he fell to his knees and begged me not to go, or at least to wait a day or two before setting out.</t86> <t87>All this was very silly, but I did not feel comfortable.</t87> <t88>However, there was a job to be done, and I could not allow anything to hinder it.</t88> <t89>So, I tried to get him to his feet and thanked him as seriously as I could, saying that my duty was inevitable and that I had to go.</t89> <t90>Upon this, he stood up, wiped his eyes, and took a cross from around his neck and gave it to me.</t90> <t91>I didn't know what to do because, as someone affiliated with the Anglican Church, I had been taught to see such things as a form of idolatry; but still, it seemed very rude to reject such a well-meaning and kind-hearted old lady.</t91> <t92>I suppose he saw the indecision on my face because he put the rosary around my neck and said, “For your mother's sake,” and left the room.</t92> <t93>I am writing this part of the journal, of course, while waiting for the delayed carriage, and the cross is still around my neck.</t93> <t94>I don't know if it's because of the old woman's fear, but I don't feel as peaceful as I usually do.</t94> <t95>If this notebook reaches Mina before I do, let it convey my farewell words to her.</t95> <t96>Here comes the carriage!</t96>
<t97>May 5.</t97> <t98>- The Castle - The grayness of the morning passed and the sun rose on the distant horizon; the horizon presented a jagged appearance with things I couldn't tell if they were trees or hills, because the horizon was so far away that big and small things had merged together.</t98> <t99>I am not sleepy, and since I won't be called until I wake up, naturally I will write until I get sleepy.</t99> <t100>There are many strange things to write about, and so that the person reading this doesn't think I had a very nice meal before leaving Bistriţa, let me write exactly what I had for dinner.</t100> <t101>I ate something called "bandit steak" - bacon, onions, and beef seasoned with red pepper, skewered and roasted over a fire in the simple London cat meat4 style!</t101> <t102>The wine was Golden Mediasch, which, though not bad on the tongue, left a strange bitterness.</t102> <t103>I only had a few glasses of it, that's all.</t103>
<t104>When I got into the carriage, the coachman had not yet taken his place, and I saw him talking with the hotel owner.</t104> <t105>It was clear that they were talking about me because they occasionally glanced at me, and some of those sitting on the bench outside the door—who were referred to by a word meaning "gossip-mongers"—went over to the two of them, eavesdropped, and then looked at me; many of them looked at me with pity.</t105> <t106>I frequently heard many strange words being repeated, as there were people of many nationalities in the crowd; so I quietly took out my multilingual dictionary from my bag and looked up those words.</t106> <t107>I must say that these words did not cheer me up at all, as they included ordog - devil, pokol - Hell, stregoica - witch, vrolok and vlkoslak - words in Slovak and Serbian respectively, meaning something like werewolf or vampire.</t107> <t108>(Note: I must ask the Count about these superstitions.</t108><t109>)</t109>
<t110>When we set out, the entire crowd gathered at the inn's door, which had grown considerably by that time, made the sign of the cross and raised two fingers towards me.</t110> <t111>With some difficulty, I managed to get one of the other travelers to explain what they meant; at first, he didn't want to answer my question, but when he learned that I was English, he explained that it was a charm or protection against the evil eye.</t111> <t112>Just as I was setting out to meet an unknown man in an unknown place, this situation did not please me at all; however, everyone was so kind-hearted, so sorrowful, and so friendly that it was impossible not to be affected.</t112> <t113>I will never forget the last glimpse of the courtyard of the inn and the crowd of interesting people making the sign of the cross around the wide arched passage, with the lush leaves of oleander and orange trees planted in green barrels behind it.</t113> <t114>Then, our coachman, whose wide linen trousers - they called them gotza - covered the entire front seat, cracked his huge whip over the four small horses harnessed abreast, and we began our journey.</t114>
<t115>If I had known the language -or rather, the languages- spoken by the other passengers, although I would not have been able to get rid of them so easily, shortly after setting off, my spiritual fears and memories related to them faded away in the beauty of the scenery.</t115><t116>In front of us stretched a green sloping terrain filled with forests, adorned here and there with steep hills, crowned with clusters of farmhouses with trees or side walls facing the road.</t116><t117>Everywhere, fruit blossoms were blooming with surprising density - apple, plum, pear, cherry; as we moved along the road, I could see that the green grass under the trees was speckled with fallen petals.</t117><t118>The road stretched out among the green hills they called "Mittelland" here, disappearing as it extended over grass-covered curves or being blocked by the edges of pine forests as it descended the slopes like tongues of flame spilling out here and there.</t118><t119>The road was rough, but still, it felt as if we were flying over it with an excited rush.</t119><t120>At that time, I did not understand what this rush meant, but it was clear that the coachman was determined not to waste any time reaching Prundu Bârgăului.</t120><t121>They said that this road was in perfect condition during the summer, but had not yet been restored after the winter snows.</t121><t122>In this respect, it is different from most of the roads passing through the Carpathian Mountains, because it is an old tradition that those roads are not kept in very good condition.</t122><t123>In the past, the hospodars did not repair the roads so that the Turks would not think they were preparing to bring in foreign troops, thus accelerating a war ready to break out at any moment.</t123>
<t124>Rising from the green hills of the Mittelland to the steep peaks of the Carpathian Mountains, large forest-covered slopes ascended.</t124> <t125>These rose on our right and left, with the evening sun fully casting its light on them, revealing the magnificent colors of these beautiful mountain ranges. Thus, in the shadows of the peaks, dark blue, purple, green where grass and rocks mingled, and brown appeared, extending into an endless depth of steep and jagged cliffs that continued until they disappeared where the snow-capped summits rose majestically in the distance.</t125> <t126>In the mountains, here and there, we saw ravines where the glint of water falling from high places was visible as the sun set.</t126> <t127>As we wound our way around the base of a hill on our serpentine path, one of my travel companions touched my arm when a great snow-covered peak appeared directly in front of us:</t127>
<t128>"Look! Isten szek!" -"The seat of God!"- and he crossed himself with a reverent expression.</t128> <t129>As we wound our way on our seemingly endless journey and the sun sank lower behind us, the shadows of evening began to silently envelop us.</t129> <t130>This was further accentuated by the snowy peak of the mountain, which still held the sunset upon it, appearing to glow with a light and cool pinkness.</t130> <t131>From time to time, we passed by Czechs and Slovaks in remarkable attire, and unfortunately, I noticed that goiter was a common condition.</t131> <t132>There were numerous crosses by the roadside, and as we passed them, all my companions crossed themselves.</t132> <t133>Here and there, there were peasant women and men kneeling before a shrine, who did not turn to look as we approached, wholly absorbed in their devotions, their eyes and ears shut off from the outside world.</t133> <t134>Many things were new to me: for instance, the haystacks in the trees, the beautiful clusters of birch trees with white trunks shimmering like silver among the delicate green of their leaves.</t134> <t135>From time to time, we overtook a leiter-wagon, an ordinary peasant cart with a long, sinuous spine adjusted to the roughness of the road.</t135> <t136>On this vehicle, there was always a group of farmers returning home, with white sheepskins for the Czechs and colored ones for the Slovaks; the second group carried long sticks with axes at the end, giving them the air of spears.</t136> <t137>As evening fell, the air grew very cold, and the increasingly dense twilight seemed to blend into a thick mist with the dimness of trees like oak, beech, and pine; yet, as we descended from the pass into the valleys deep between the arms of the mountains, we occasionally came across dark fir trees backed by patches of snow that had not yet melted.</t137> <t138>At times, the road was intersected by pine forests that seemed to close in on us in the darkness, while the early evening sunset, sinking ever lower, cast strange shapes with the ghostly clouds drifting incessantly through the valleys of the Carpathians, creating an unusually eerie and majestic effect with the large patches of gray covering the trees here and there.</t138> <t139>Sometimes the hills were so steep that, despite our driver's haste, the horses could only proceed slowly.</t139> <t140>I wanted to get out of the carriage and approach the horses, but the driver would not allow it.</t140> <t141>"No, no!" he said; "You must not walk here; the dogs are too savage," then, making it clear he was making a cruel joke by looking around to catch the approving smiles of the others, he added, "You will have enough of such matters to deal with before you sleep." </t141> <t142>The only stop he made was for a moment to light the lamps.</t142>
<t143>It seemed as if an excitement had arisen among the passengers when it got dark, and the passengers kept talking to the coachman one after another, as if insisting that he go faster.</t143><t144>The coachman mercilessly whipped the horses with his long whip and urged them to make more effort with encouraging wild shouts.</t144><t145>Then, in the middle of the darkness ahead, as if there was a gap between the hills, I saw a piece of some kind of gray light.</t145><t146>The excitement of the passengers grew even more; the frenzied coach was shaking on its large leather springs and swaying from side to side like a ship tossed in a stormy sea.</t146><t147>I had to hold on.</t147><t148>The road improved a bit, and it seemed as if we were flying forward.</t148><t149>Then, it seemed as if the mountains on either side were closing in on us; we were entering the Borgo Pass.</t149><t150>Most of the passengers, with an insistence that accepted no objections, individually offered me gifts; of course, they were unusual and strange in nature, but each was given with good intentions, a kind word, a prayer, and a mixture of those strange gestures of fear I had seen outside the hotel in Bistriţa (cross and protection against the evil eye).</t150><t151>Later, as we were flying forward, the driver leaned forward and the passengers on either side stretched their necks out from the edge of the coach, eagerly and attentively looking into the darkness.</t151><t152>It was clear that something very exciting was happening or expected, but despite asking each passenger, no one gave me the slightest explanation.</t152><t153>This state of excitement continued for a short while; finally, we saw the pass opening up ahead of us to the east.</t153><t154>There was a heavy and oppressive storm feeling in the air, with black clouds rolling over our heads.</t154><t155>The mountain ranges seemed to have separated the two airs, and we had entered the stormy one.</t155><t156>Now I too was looking out to see the vehicle that would take me to the Count.</t156><t157>I was expecting to see the brightness of the lamps from the darkness at any moment; but everywhere was dark.</t157><t158>The only light was the flickering light of our own coach, which showed the steam from the breath of our rapidly running horses like a white cloud.</t158><t159>Now, we could see the sandy road stretching out white in front of us, but there was no sign of any vehicle.</t159><t160>The passengers, seeming to mock my disappointment, sighed with satisfaction and leaned back.</t160><t161>When the driver looked at his watch and said something to the others that I could barely hear, I had already started thinking about what I should do; these words were spoken so quietly, in such a low tone, that I think he said: "An hour early."</t161><t162>Then, turning to me, he said in German even worse than mine:</t162>
<t163>“There is no car here.</t163> <t164>After all, Herr is not expected.</t164> <t165>He will now go to Bukovina and return here tomorrow or the day after; the best is the day after tomorrow.”</t165> <t166>As he spoke, the horses began to neigh, snort from their noses, and lunge forward wildly; so much so that the driver had to restrain them.</t166> <t167>Then, amidst the villagers' chorus of cries and the universal gesture of crossing themselves, a light carriage with four horses came up the hill behind us, caught up to us, and began to move alongside our carriage.</t167> <t168>As beams of light fell on them, in the light emitted by our lamps, I saw that the horses were coal black and magnificent animals.</t168> <t169>The carriage was driven by a tall man with a long brown beard that hid his face and a large black hat.</t169> <t170>I could only see the gleam of a very bright pair of eyes, which appeared red under the lamp light as they turned towards us.</t170> <t171>He said to the driver:</t171>
<t172>“You're early tonight, my friend.”</t172> <t173>The man replied, stuttering:</t173>
<t174>“The English gentleman was in a hurry.”</t174> <t175>In response, the foreigner said:</t175>
<t176>“I guess that's why you wanted him to continue to Bukovina.</t176> <t177>You can't fool me, my friend; I know a lot and my horses are fast.”</t177> <t178>He was smiling as he spoke, and the light of the lamp fell upon a hard mouth with overly red lips and sharp-looking, ivory white teeth.</t178> <t179>One of my fellow travelers whispered a line from Bürger's "Lenore" to another:</t179>
<t180>For the dead travel fast.</t180> <t181>-</t181>
<t182>(Because the dead travel fast.</t182><t183>)</t183>
<t184>The strange driver had clearly heard the words, as he looked up with a gleaming smile.</t184> <t185>The passenger turned his face away while making the sign of the cross with two fingers.</t185> <t186>"Give me Herr's luggage," said the driver; my bags were handed over with great enthusiasm and placed in the carriage.</t186> <t187>Then, as the other carriage was nearby, I transferred from our carriage to the other without stepping on the ground, with the coachman helping me with a grip as strong as steel; he must have had tremendous strength.</t187> <t188>Without a word, he flicked the reins, the horses turned, and we swiftly advanced into the darkness of the pass.</t188> <t189>When I looked back, I saw the steam rising from the post coach's horses and the silhouettes of my former travel companions making the sign of the cross under the light of the lamps.</t189> <t190>Then, the driver cracked his whip, called out to his horses, and they swiftly set off towards Bukovina.</t190>
<t191>As they were engulfed in darkness, I felt a strange shiver and loneliness descended upon me; however, a cloak was thrown over my shoulders and a blanket over my knees, and the coachman said in perfect German:</t191>
<t192>“The night is cold, mein Herr, and my master Count has ordered me to take every care of you.”</t192> <t193>“If you wish, there is a flask of slivovitz [local plum brandy] under the seat.”</t193> <t194>I did not drink, but knowing it was there was comforting.</t194> <t195>I was feeling a bit strange and quite scared.</t195> <t196>If there had been another option, I think I would have chosen it rather than continue this unknown night journey.</t196> <t197>The carriage was moving straight ahead at a fast pace, then we made a complete turn and proceeded along another straight path.</t197> <t198>It seemed to me as if we were passing the same place over and over again; therefore, I paid attention to a noticeable spot and realized that this was indeed the case.</t198> <t199>I wished I had asked the coachman what all this meant, but I was really afraid to do so because, given my position, I thought that any objection, if there was a desire for delay, would have no effect.</t199> <t200>But a little later, wondering how time was passing, I struck a match and looked at my watch by its flame; it was a few minutes to midnight.</t200> <t201>This shocked me somewhat, because I suppose the general superstitions about midnight had been reinforced by my recent experiences.</t201>
<t202>Then, somewhere far down the road, a dog began to howl at the farmhouse - a long, painful wail as if from fear.</t202><t203>The sound was taken up by another dog, then another, and another; until now, carried by the wind softly moaning through the pass, a wild howling began from every part of the country, from the gloom of the night, in every corner that the imagination could grasp.</t203><t204>At the first howl, the horses became tense and started to rear, but they calmed down when the driver spoke to them soothingly, though they were trembling and sweating as if they had escaped from a sudden fear.</t204><t205>Then, in the distance, a higher and sharper howl - the howl of wolves - began in the mountains on either side of us, and this howl affected the horses and me in the same way, for while I was thinking of jumping out of the carriage and fleeing, they reared once more and dashed forward madly; so much so that the driver had to use all his tremendous strength to prevent them from bolting away.</t205><t206>However, within a few minutes, my ears got used to the sound, and during this time, the horses calmed down enough for the driver to get down and stand in front of them.</t206><t207>The driver petted them, soothed them, and whispered something into their ears, as I have heard horse trainers do, with an extraordinary effect, for with his caressing, the horses, though still trembling, became quite docile once again.</t207><t208>The driver took his place again and set off at great speed, shaking the reins.</t208><t209>This time, after reaching the far corner of the pass, he suddenly turned onto a narrow road that sharply curved to the right.</t209>
<t210>Before long, we were surrounded by trees; in some places, they leaned over the road as if we were passing through a tunnel; once again, huge frowning rocks on both sides were bravely protecting us.</t210> <t211>Even though we were in a sheltered area, we could hear the increasing sound of the wind; because the wind was moaning and howling between the rocks, and as we moved quickly, the branches of the trees were hitting each other.</t211> <t212>The air grew colder and beautiful, powdery snow began to fall; so much so that soon we and everything around us were covered with a white blanket.</t212> <t213>The sharp wind still carried the howling of the dogs; although the sounds were getting fainter as we progressed on our way.</t213> <t214>The howling of the wolves was coming closer and closer, as if they were surrounding us from every direction.</t214> <t215>I had begun to get terribly scared, and the horses shared my fear; but the coachman was not disturbed at all.</t215> <t216>He continued to turn his head from side to side, but I couldn't see anything in the dark.</t216>
<t217>Suddenly, I saw a faint, flickering blue flame ahead on our left.</t217> <t218>At the same moment, the driver saw it too; he immediately stopped the horses, got down, and disappeared into the darkness.</t218> <t219>I didn't know what to do, and as the howling of the wolves got closer, I became even more indecisive; but just as I was thinking about this, the driver suddenly reappeared, took his place without saying a word, and we continued our journey.</t219> <t220>I think I must have fallen asleep and continued seeing this event in my dream because it seemed to repeat endlessly; now, looking back, everything seems like a terrible nightmare.</t220> <t221>At one point, the flame appeared so close to the road that despite the surrounding darkness, I could observe the driver's movements.</t221> <t222>He quickly went to where the flame was - the flame must have been quite faint because it didn't seem to illuminate the area around it - and he gathered some stones and arranged them in a certain way.</t222> <t223>Once, a strange optical illusion occurred: When the driver stood between me and the flame, he didn't block it, because despite him standing between us, I could see the ghostly shape of the flames.</t223> <t224>This situation startled me, but since the image was fleeting, I concluded that my eyes were straining in the dark and deceiving me.</t224> <t225>Then, for a while, no blue flame was seen and we quickly moved into the darkness, surrounded by the howling of wolves that seemed to be following us in a moving circle.</t225>
<t226>Finally, there came a moment when the driver went farther than he had ever gone before, and in his absence, the horses began to tremble worse than ever and to snort and neigh in fear.</t226> <t227>I could see no reason for this, as the howling of the wolves had completely ceased; but just then, the moon, gliding through the dark clouds, appeared behind the rugged ridge of a pine-covered rock, and by its light, I saw a circle of wolves surrounding us, with their white teeth, red tongues hanging out of their mouths, long strong legs, and coarse fur.</t227> <t228>In the relentless silence that had fallen upon them, they were a hundred times more terrifying than when they howled.</t228> <t229>As for me, I felt as if I were paralyzed with fear.</t229> <t230>One can only truly understand the real meaning of such terrifying things when confronted with them.</t230>
<t231>As if the moonlight had a special effect on them, the wolves suddenly started howling.</t231> <t232>The horses jumped here and there, rearing up and looking around in despair with pitiable eyes; but the living ring of terror surrounded them from every direction, and they were inevitably trapped within the circle.</t232> <t233>I called out to the coachman to come, because it seemed our only chance was to break out of the circle and help the man come to us.</t233> <t234>I shouted and struck the side of the carriage, hoping that the noise would scare the wolves in that direction and give the coachman a chance to realize the trap we had fallen into.</t234> <t235>I don't know how he got there, but I heard the coachman's voice rise in a commanding tone, and when I looked towards the sound, I saw him standing on the road.</t235> <t236>When he waved his long arms as if pushing aside an intangible barrier, the wolves retreated, far back.</t236> <t237>At that moment, a heavy cloud passed in front of the moon, and thus we were plunged into darkness again.</t237>
<t238>When I was able to see again, the driver was climbing into the car and the wolves had disappeared from sight.</t238> <t239>All of this was so strange and so eerie that a terrible fear descended upon me; I was afraid to speak or move.</t239> <t240>As we continued on our path, which was plunged into complete darkness because the rolling clouds hid the moon, time seemed to stand still.</t240> <t241>We kept climbing; although there were occasional steep descents, we were mostly ascending.</t241> <t242>Suddenly, I became aware of the fact that the driver was stopping the horses in the courtyard of a very large and ruined castle, whose high, dark windows let in no light and whose broken battlements formed a jagged line against the moonlit sky.</t242>
<t243>II</t243>
<t244>Jonathan Harker's journal (continued)</t244>
<t245>May 5.</t245> <t246>- I must have fallen asleep because if I were fully awake, I would have noticed approaching such a remarkable place.</t246> <t247>In the dark, the courtyard appeared considerably wide, and it might have seemed larger than it actually was because many dark paths opened under the large, round arches.</t247> <t248>I have not yet seen the courtyard in daylight.</t248>
<t249>When the car stopped, the driver jumped down and extended his hand to help me get out.</t249> <t250>Once again, I couldn't help but notice his tremendous strength.</t250> <t251>Honestly, his hand was like a steel clamp that could crush mine if he wanted to.</t251> <t252>Then, he took my suitcases out of the car and placed them beside me on the ground as I stood by the large door located at the protruding entrance made of old, iron-nailed, and magnificent stone.</t252> <t253>Even in the dim light, I could see that the stone was impressively adorned with carvings, but it had been quite worn down by time and weather.</t253> <t254>As I stood there, the driver jumped back into his seat and shook the reins; the horses set off and, along with the car, disappeared into one of the dark gates.</t254>
<t255>I stood silently where I was, because I didn't know what to do.</t255> <t256>There wasn't a single trace of a bell or door knocker around; it was unlikely that my voice would pierce through these frowning walls and dark window gaps.</t256> <t257>The time I spent waiting seemed endless, and I felt doubts and fears swarming over me.</t257> <t258>What kind of place had I come to, what kind of people was I among?</t258> <t259>What a dreadful adventure had I embarked upon?</t259> <t260>Was this a usual event in the life of a solicitor's clerk sent to explain the purchase of a property in London to a foreigner?</t260> <t261>Solicitor's clerk!</t261> <t262>Mina would not have liked this at all.</t262> <t263>Solicitor, because I had learned that I had passed the exam just before leaving London; but was I now truly a solicitor?</t263> <t264>I began to rub my eyes and pinch myself to see if I was awake.</t264> <t265>All of this seemed like a terrible nightmare to me, and just like after a day when I had overworked, I hoped to suddenly wake up and find myself at home with the dawn trying to enter through the window.</t265> <t266>But my body responded to the pinching test, and my eyes were not deceiving me.</t266> <t267>I was indeed awake and in the Carpathian Mountains.</t267> <t268>Now, all I had to do was be patient and wait for the morning to come.</t268>
<t269>Just as I had reached this conclusion, I heard the sound of heavy footsteps approaching from behind the large door and saw the glimmer of light coming through the cracks.</t269> <t270>Then came the sound of clinking chains and bolts being drawn.</t270> <t271>With a creak caused by long disuse, a key turned in the lock and the large door opened inward.</t271>
<t272>Inside, a tall, elderly man was standing; apart from his long white mustache, he was clean-shaven and dressed entirely in black without a hint of color.</t272> <t273>He was holding an antique silver lamp; although it had neither a lamp chimney nor a globe, a flame was burning inside it, and as the flame flickered in the draft from the open door, it cast long, trembling shadows.</t273> <t274>With a rather respectful gesture of his right hand, he invited me in and said in a peculiar intonation, yet in perfect English:</t274>
<t275>"Welcome to my home!</t275> <t276>Enter freely and of your own will!" He did not make the slightest move to greet me; he stood like a statue, as if the act of welcoming had turned him to stone.</t276> <t277>However, the moment I crossed the threshold, he suddenly lunged forward and extended his hand, gripping mine with a force that made me want to pull back - so much so that this effect was not diminished in the least by the fact that his hand was ice-cold.</t277> <t278>He then said again:</t278>
<t279>“Welcome to my home.</t279> <t280>Enter freely.</t280> <t281>Go freely and leave behind some of the happiness you bring!” The strength in the handshake was so similar to that of the driver whose face I had not seen that I doubted for a moment whether I was speaking to the same person; therefore, to be sure, I asked questioningly, “Count Dracula?”</t281> <t282>He bowed respectfully as he replied:</t282>
<t283>“Dracula says, welcome to my home and to you, Mr. Harker.</t283> <t284>Come inside; the night air is cold; you must need something to eat and rest.”</t284> <t285>As he spoke, he placed the lamp on a shelf on the wall and went outside to get my luggage; before I could act quickly and stop him, he had already carried my luggage inside.</t285> <t286>I objected but he insisted:</t286>
<t287>“No, sir, you are my guest.</t287> <t288>It is late and my men are not here.</t288> <t289>Allow me to ensure your comfort myself.”</t289> <t290>He insisted on carrying my belongings along the corridor and then through another wide corridor where our footsteps echoed loudly on the grand spiral staircase and stone floor.</t290> <t291>At the end of this corridor, he opened a heavy door, and I was very pleased to see that a table was set for dinner inside and large logs were blazing in the magnificent fireplace.</t291>
<t292>The Count put my bags down, closed the door, and walked to the other end of the room, opening another door that led to a small, octagonal room illuminated by a single lamp and seemingly devoid of any windows.</t292> <t293>He passed through this door, opened another one, and gestured for me to enter with his hand.</t293> <t294>It was a pleasant sight; for here, there was a large bedroom well-lit and warmed by logs in a wide fireplace that seemed to roar with flames.</t294> <t295>The Count placed my suitcases inside and stepped back; before closing the door, he said:</t295>
<t296>“After your journey, you need to refresh yourself by changing your clothes.</t296> <t297>I believe you will find everything you desire.</t297> <t298>When you are ready, come to the other room where you will find your meal prepared.</t298><t299>”</t299>
<t300>The brightness, warmth, and the Count's refined welcome seemed to have dispelled all my doubts and fears.</t300> <t301>When I returned to my usual state of mind, I realized that I was almost fainting from hunger; so I quickly got dressed and went to the other room.</t301>
<t302>I saw that the meal had already been prepared.</t302> <t303>My host, standing by the stone carvings of the large fireplace, made a slight hand gesture towards the table and said:</t303>
<t304>“Please sit down and eat as you wish.</t304> <t305>I hope you won't mind that I am not joining you; I have already had my dinner and do not eat at this late hour.</t305><t306>”</t306>
<t307>I handed the sealed letter entrusted to me by Mr. Hawkins to the Count.</t307> <t308>He opened the letter and read it with a serious expression; then, with a pleasant smile, he handed it to me to read.</t308> <t309>At least one paragraph gave me a joyful excitement:</t309>
<t310>I am deeply saddened that the recurrence of gout, a chronic ailment I suffer from, has definitively prevented me from traveling for a while; however, I am pleased to say that I have been able to send someone in my place whom I trust in every possible way and who is highly qualified.</t310> <t311>A young man with his own unique energy and talent, possessing an honest nature.</t311> <t312>He is discreet and quiet, and has been trained in my service.</t312> <t313>During his stay with you, he will be ready to accompany you whenever you wish and will take your instructions in all matters.</t313>
<t314>The Count personally stepped forward, lifted the lid off one of the dishes, and immediately, I began to eat a perfectly roasted chicken.</t314> <t315>My meal consisted of this chicken, some cheese, salad, and a bottle of old Tokay from which I drank two glasses.</t315> <t316>While I was eating, the Count asked many questions about my journey, and I gradually recounted my experiences to him.</t316>
<t317>During this time, I had finished my meal, and at the request of my host, I pulled a chair up to the fire and began to smoke a cigar he offered, apologizing for not joining me.</t317> <t318>Now I had the opportunity to observe him, and I saw that he had quite a remarkable physiognomy.</t318>
<t319>His face, with the high arch of his thin nose and unusually curved nostrils, high-domed forehead, and hair sparse at the temples but plentiful elsewhere, resembled a strong - very strong - eagle.</t319> <t320>His eyebrows were so thick that they almost met above his nose, and they seemed to curl within their own abundance.</t320> <t321>As far as I could see from under his thick mustache, his mouth appeared stern and quite cruel with unusually sharp white teeth; the teeth extended over lips that showed a surprising sign of life for a man of his age, being a vivid red.</t321> <t322>As for the rest; his ears were pale and excessively pointed at the tops; his chin was broad and strong, and his cheeks, though thin, were firm.</t322> <t323>The overall effect he created was one of extraordinary pallor.</t323>
<t324>Until then, I had noticed the backs of his hands under the light cast by the fire, they looked quite white and beautiful; but now, seeing them up close, I couldn't help but notice how coarse they were - they had stubby fingers and were broad.</t324> <t325>Strangely enough, there was hair in the middle of his palm.</t325> <t326>His nails were long and thin, and their tips were sharply cut.</t326> <t327>I couldn't help but shudder when the Count leaned over and touched me with his hands.</t327> <t328>It might have been because his breath smelled, but I was suffering from a terrible nausea that I couldn't hide no matter what I did.</t328> <t329>Clearly noticing this, the Count withdrew; with a cruel smile that showed his protruding teeth more than ever before, he sat down on the other side of the fireplace once again.</t329> <t330>For a while, we both remained silent; when I looked out the window, I saw the first pale signs of the approaching dawn.</t330> <t331>It seemed as if a strange calm had settled over everything; but when I listened closely, I heard the howling of many wolves, seemingly coming from down the valley.</t331> <t332>The Count's eyes gleamed and he said:</t332>
<t333>“Listen to them - the children of the night.</t333> <t334>What music they make!" I think he saw a strange expression on my face, so he added:</t334>
<t335>“Ah, sir, you city folks cannot understand the feelings of a hunter.”</t335> <t336>Then he stood up and said:</t336>
<t337>“But you must be tired.</t337> <t338>Your room is ready and you can sleep as long as you want tomorrow.</t338> <t339>I need to be outside until the afternoon; so sleep well and have sweet dreams!” And with a respectful bow, he opened the door of the octagonal room for me, I entered my room...</t339>
<t340>I am completely in a sea of bewilderment.</t340> <t341>I have doubts; I am afraid; I am thinking of strange things that I do not even have the courage to reveal to my own soul.</t341> <t342>My God, protect me, if only for the sake of my loved ones!</t342>
<t343>May 7.</t343> <t344>- It's early morning again, but I am rested and have enjoyed the last twenty-four hours.</t344> <t345>I slept until late in the day and woke up whenever I wanted.</t345> <t346>When I got dressed, I went to the room where we had dinner and saw that a cold breakfast was ready with coffee kept warm by a pot left on the fire.</t346> <t347>There was a card on the table with the following written on it:</t347>
<t348>I won't be around here for a while.</t348> <t349>Don't wait for me.</t349> <t350>- D.</t350>
<t351>Thus, I began to eat and enjoyed the taste of a hearty meal.</t351> <t352>When I finished my meal, I looked for a bell to inform the servants that I was done; but I couldn't find one.</t352> <t353>Considering the extraordinary displays of wealth around me, there were undoubtedly strange deficiencies in the house.</t353> <t354>The tableware was made of gold and so beautifully crafted that it must be of immense value.</t354> <t355>The curtains on the windows, the upholstery of the chairs and sofas, and the drapes surrounding my bed were made of the most expensive and beautiful fabrics; they must have been of tremendous value when produced, because despite being in perfect condition, they were undoubtedly centuries old.</t355> <t356>I had seen something similar at Hampton Court Palace, but they were worn, frayed, and moth-eaten.</t356> <t357>Yet, there were no mirrors in any room.</t357> <t358>There wasn't even a dressing mirror on my table, and before shaving or brushing my hair, I had to take out the small shaving mirror from my bag.</t358> <t359>I have not seen any servants anywhere nor heard any sound near the castle except for the howling of wolves.</t359> <t360>When I finished my meal - I don't know whether to call it breakfast or dinner, as it was between five and six o'clock when I ate - I looked for something to read, as I did not want to explore the castle without the Count's permission.</t360> <t361>There was absolutely nothing in the room; no books, no newspapers, not even writing materials; so I opened another door in the room and found a sort of library.</t361> <t362>I tried the door opposite mine but found it was locked.</t362>
<t363>In the library, with joy, I found a large number of English books; all the shelves were filled with these books, and there were bound magazines and newspapers.</t363> <t364>English magazines and newspapers were scattered on a table in the middle, but none of them were recent.</t364> <t365>The books were of very different genres - history, geography, politics, economic policy, botany, geology, law - all about England and the English lifestyle, traditions, and customs.</t365> <t366>There were even reference sources like the "London Guide", the "Red" and "Blue" books, the "Whitaker Almanac", the "Army and Navy Lists" - which somehow delighted me upon seeing - and the "Law List".</t366>
<t367>While I was looking at the books, the door opened and the Count entered.</t367> <t368>He greeted me warmly and, after saying he hoped I had rested well during the night, he continued:</t368>
<t369>“I am glad you found your way here, because I am sure there are many things here that will interest you.</t369> <t370>These friends,” -he placed his hand on some books- “have been my good companions, and over the past few years, since I decided to go to London, they have given me many, many pleasant hours.</t370> <t371>Through them, I have come to know your great England; to know it is to love it.</t371> <t372>I am eager to walk through the crowded streets of your dear London, to stand amidst the hustle and bustle of humanity, to share in the city's life, its changes, its death, and everything that makes it what it is.</t372> <t373>But alas!</t373> <t374>I know your language only through books.</t374> <t375>I rely on you, my friend, to speak this language.</t375><t376>”</t376>
<t377>"But Mr. Kant," I said, "you know and speak English perfectly!" He nodded solemnly.</t377>
<t378>“Thank you for your flattering compliment, my friend, but I am afraid I am still at the very beginning of the journey I must undertake.</t378> <t379>Yes, it is true that I know the grammar and the words, but I still do not know how to pronounce them.</t379><t380>”</t380>

<t383>“It is not so,” he replied.</t383> <t384>“Yes, I know that if I moved to your London and spoke there, no one would fail to understand that I am a foreigner.</t384> <t385>That is not enough for me.</t385> <t386>Here I am noble; I am a boyar, ordinary people know me, and I am a master.</t386> <t387>But in a foreign country, a foreigner is nobody; people do not know him, and not knowing is not caring.</t387> <t388>If I am like the others, that is, if no one stops to look at me or, in the middle of a conversation, upon hearing my words, stops to say, ‘Ha, ha! A foreigner!,’ I would be content with that.</t388> <t389>I have been a master for a long time, and I will continue to be a master, or at least no one else should be my master.</t389> <t390>You did not come here merely to talk about my new property in London as an agent of my friend Peter Hawkins of Exeter.</t390> <t391>I hope you will stay with me for a while, so that through our conversations I can learn the English accent; I ask you to tell me when I make a mistake during my speech - even the slightest mistake.</t391> <t392>I apologize for having to be away for so long today; but I know you will forgive someone who has so many important matters at hand.</t392><t393>”</t393>

<t396>“You can go anywhere you want in the castle; except for the places where the doors are locked and, of course, the places you wouldn't want to go.</t396> <t397>There is a reason why everything is the way it is right now, and if you saw and knew what I know, you might understand better.”</t397> <t398>I said that I was sure of this.</t398> <t399>He then continued his words:</t399>

<t414>"Ama," I said, "how could it have remained undiscovered for so long when there is such clear evidence that people would see if they took the trouble to look?"</t414> <t415>The Count laughed, and as his lips drew back from his gums, his long, sharp canine teeth appeared in a strange manner; he answered my question as follows:</t415>
<t416>“Because your villager is actually cowardly and stupid!</t416> <t417>Those flames appear only one night.</t417> <t418>And on that night, no one in this country, if they can help it, steps outside their home.</t418> <t419>And sir, if they do step out, they wouldn't know what to do.</t419> <t420>Even the villager who said the flame marked the spot wouldn't know where to look for his own mark in daylight.</t420> <t421>I'll bet you wouldn't be able to find those places again either, would you?</t421><t422>”</t422>
