<t1489>We sat with Lucy for a while; the view in front of us was so beautiful that we held hands while sitting; she told me again and again about Arthur and their upcoming marriage.</t1489> <t1490>This saddened me a bit, because I haven't heard from Jonathan for a whole month.</t1490>
<t1491>The same day.</t1491> <t1492>- I came here alone because I am very sad.</t1492> <t1493>There was no letter for me.</t1493> <t1494>I hope Jonathan has no problems.</t1494> <t1495>The clock has just struck nine.</t1495> <t1496>I see lights scattered over the whole town; sometimes in rows along the roads and sometimes individually; to the right, they stretch along the Esk and disappear in the curve of the valley.</t1496> <t1497>The view to my left is cut off by a black line belonging to the roof of the house next to the Monastery.</t1497> <t1498>Sheep and lambs are bleating in the distant fields behind me, and the clatter of a donkey's hooves can be heard from the asphalt road.</t1498> <t1499>The band on the pier is playing a fast, ear-piercing waltz, and in one of the back streets beyond the pier, a Salvation Army meeting is being held.</t1499> <t1500>Neither band can hear the other, but here, up above, I can hear and see both.</t1500> <t1501>I wonder where Jonathan is and if he is thinking of me!</t1501> <t1502>I wish he were here.</t1502>
<t1503>Dr. Seward's journal</t1503>
<t1504>June 5.</t1504> <t1505>- The Renfield case is becoming more interesting as I understand the man better.</t1505> <t1506>He has some highly developed qualities: Selfishness, reticence, and determination.</t1506> <t1507>I wish I could understand what his determination is directed towards.</t1507> <t1508>He seems to have a specific plan of his own, but I don't know what it is yet.</t1508> <t1509>His love for animals is one of his good qualities, but he has such unprecedented tendencies in this regard that sometimes I think the man possesses a mere perverse cruelty.</t1509> <t1510>He has peculiar kinds of pets.</t1510> <t1511>His current hobby is catching flies.</t1511> <t1512>At present, he has so many flies that I felt compelled to personally warn him.</t1512> <t1513>I was surprised to see that, as I expected, he did not have a fit of rage and took the matter seriously.</t1513> <t1514>He thought for a moment and said, “Will you give me three days?</t1514> <t1515>I will get rid of them.”</t1515> <t1516>Of course, I said I could give him that time.</t1516> <t1517>I must keep an eye on him.</t1517>
<t1518>June 18.</t1518> <t1519>- Now he is obsessed with spiders and has a box full of large spiders.</t1519> <t1520>He feeds them with flies, and although he uses half of his food to attract flies outside his room, the number of flies has reasonably decreased.</t1520>
<t1521>July 1st.</t1521> <t1522>- Now the spiders have also become as big a problem as the flies, and today I told him that he needed to get rid of the spiders.</t1522> <t1523>He was very upset to hear this, so I told him that he still needed to clean up some of them.</t1523> <t1524>He accepted this cheerfully, and I gave him the same amount of time I had given him before to reduce their number.</t1524> <t1525>When I was with him, he made me feel terribly nauseous, because when a disgusting, bloated horsefly that had been feeding on rotting meat buzzed into the room, he caught it, held it joyfully between his fingers for a few seconds, and before I could understand what he was going to do, he put it in his mouth and ate it.</t1525> <t1526>I scolded him for this, but he calmly argued that the fly was very tasty and nutritious; that it meant life - strong life - and it gave him life.</t1526> <t1527>This gave me an idea or at least a fragment of an idea.</t1527> <t1528>I should watch how he gets rid of his spiders.</t1528> <t1529>Clearly, he has a serious problem in his mind, because he keeps a notebook in which he constantly scribbles something.</t1529> <t1530>As if, in the words of the auditors, he is "focusing" on an account, all the pages of the notebook are filled with heaps of numbers, mostly gathered in clusters, consisting of odd numbers; then he adds these totals together in clusters as well.</t1530>
<t1531>8 July.</t1531> <t1532>- There is a method in his madness, and the thought in my mind is maturing.</t1532> <t1533>Soon it will be a complete idea, and then, oh, unconscious thought!</t1533> <t1534>You must bow to your conscious brother.</t1534> <t1535>I stayed away from my friend for a few days so that I might notice if there was any difference.</t1535> <t1536>Everything was the same except that he had parted with some of his pets and acquired a new one.</t1536> <t1537>He had managed to obtain a sparrow and had even partially tamed it.</t1537> <t1538>The method of taming was simple, for the number of spiders had already decreased.</t1538> <t1539>The remaining ones were well-fed, for he was still offering them the flies he lured with his food.</t1539>
<t1540>July 19.</t1540> <t1541>- We are making progress.</t1541> <t1542>My friend now has a complete sparrow colony, and with it, the flies and spiders are almost entirely gone.</t1542> <t1543>When I entered, he ran to me and said he wanted a big favor - a very, very big favor; he was fawning like a dog as he spoke.</t1543> <t1544>I asked him what he wanted; with a kind of ecstasy in his voice and manner, he said:</t1544>
<t1545>“A kitten, a small, cute, shiny-furred, playful kitten that I can play with, train, and feed -and feed- and feed.”</t1545> <t1546>I was not caught unprepared by this request, as I had noticed that the size and liveliness of her pets were increasing, but I did not care about the fact that the lovely pet sparrow family would be gone in the same way as flies and spiders; therefore, I said I would take care of it and asked if she wanted an adult cat instead of a kitten.</t1546> <t1547>Her greed betrayed her as she replied:</t1547>
<t1548>“Ah! Yes! I want a cat!</t1548> <t1549>The only reason I asked for a kitten was to see if you would refuse to give me one.</t1549> <t1550>No one refuses to give me a kitten, right?”</t1550> <t1551>I shook my head and said that I was afraid it was impossible at the moment but that I would look into it.</t1551> <t1552>His face fell; I could see an expression of danger warning on his face because suddenly, a merciless, sideways glance that meant killing settled on his face.</t1552> <t1553>This man is an undeveloped, homicidal maniac.</t1553> <t1554>I must test him with his current request and see how it turns out; then I will learn more.</t1554>
<t1555>It's 10 PM.</t1555> <t1556>- I visited him once more and found him sitting in a corner, lost in deep thoughts.</t1556> <t1557>When I entered, he knelt before me and begged for permission to feed a cat; he said his salvation depended on it.</t1557> <t1558>However, I was determined and told him he couldn't feed the cat, upon which he walked away without a word and went to the corner where I had found him, sitting and gnawing at his fingers.</t1558> <t1559>I will see him early tomorrow morning.</t1559>
<t1560>July 20.</t1560> <t1561>- Before the nurse made her morning rounds, I visited Renfield very early in the morning.</t1561> <t1562>I found him standing, humming a tune.</t1562> <t1563>He was spreading the sugar he had collected on the window and was clearly starting to catch flies again; he began this task with joy and a certain dignity.</t1563> <t1564>I looked around for his birds and, not seeing them, asked where they were.</t1564> <t1565>Without turning around, he said they had all flown away.</t1565> <t1566>There were a few scattered feathers in the room and a drop of blood on his pillow.</t1566> <t1567>I didn't say anything, but I went to the nurse and told her to inform me if there was anything unusual about Renfield during the day.</t1567>
<t1568>It's 11 in the morning.</t1568> <t1569>- The nurse came to me to say that Renfield was very sick and had vomited a ton of bird feathers.</t1569> <t1570>“I think, doctor,” he said, “he ate his birds, took his birds and ate them raw!</t1570><t1571>”</t1571>
<t1572>It's 11 PM.</t1572> <t1573>- This evening, I gave Renfield a sedative strong enough to make him sleep and took his small notebook to examine.</t1573> <t1574>The thought that was ringing in my head has recently been completed, and my theory has been proven.</t1574> <t1575>My homicidal lunatic is of a special kind.</t1575> <t1576>I need to discover a new classification for him and define him as a zoophagous (life-eating) lunatic; what he desires is to absorb as much life as possible and to work tirelessly to do so in an accumulative manner.</t1576> <t1577>He gave many flies to a spider, many spiders to a bird, and then asked for a cat to eat many birds.</t1577> <t1578>What could his next steps be?</t1578> <t1579>It was almost worth completing the experiment.</t1579> <t1580>If there were sufficient justification, it could be done.</t1580> <t1581>People ridicule experiments on living beings, but look at their results today!</t1581> <t1582>Why not advance science in the most difficult and crucial aspect - the knowledge of the brain?</t1582> <t1583>If I could unlock the secret of such a brain - if I had the key to the imagination of a single lunatic - I could elevate my field of science to a pinnacle where Burdon-Sanderson's physiology and Ferrier's brain knowledge would pale in comparison.</t1583> <t1584>If only there were sufficient justification!</t1584> <t1585>I must not think about this too much, or I will be tempted; a good justification could point the needle toward me, for am I not, by nature, an extraordinary brain?</t1585>
<t1586>How beautifully humans reason!</t1586> <t1587>Madmen always do this within themselves.</t1587> <t1588>I wonder how many lives a person values, if such a thing exists.</t1588> <t1589>He closed the account very accurately and started a new page today.</t1589> <t1590>How many of us start a new page in our lives every day?</t1590>
<t1591>Just yesterday, it felt as if my entire life had ended along with my new hope, and indeed, I started a new chapter.</t1591> <t1592>This will continue until the Great Judge tallies my total and closes my main ledger with a balance of profit or loss.</t1592> <t1593>Ah! Lucy! Lucy! I cannot be angry with you or with my friend whose happiness is your happiness; but all I can do is wait and work in despair.</t1593> <t1594>Work! Work!</t1594>
<t1595>I wish I had as strong a reason as my poor mad friend there; a good and selfless reason to make me work; that would truly be my happiness.</t1595>
<t1596>Mina Murray's journal</t1596>
<t1597>July 26.</t1597> <t1598>- I am anxious and expressing myself here calms me; it's like whispering to oneself and listening at the same time.</t1598> <t1599>Also, there is something in the shorthand symbols that sets it apart from regular writing.</t1599> <t1600>I am unhappy about Lucy and Jonathan.</t1600> <t1601>I hadn't heard from Jonathan for a while and was worried; but yesterday, dear Mr. Hawkins, who has always been very kind, sent me a letter from him.</t1601> <t1602>I had asked him if he had heard from Jonathan, and he said that the enclosed letter had just arrived.</t1602> <t1603>The letter was a single line written from Castle Dracula, saying that he was about to set off for home.</t1603> <t1604>This is not at all like Jonathan; I don't understand anything and this situation makes me very uneasy.</t1604> <t1605>Then, Lucy, although she is actually very well, has recently reverted to her old sleepwalking habit.</t1605> <t1606>Her mother told me about this and we decided to lock our room door every night.</t1606> <t1607>Mrs. Westenra thinks that sleepwalkers always go up to the roofs of houses, walk along the edges of cliffs, and then suddenly wake up and fall down with a desperate scream that echoes everywhere.</t1607> <t1608>Poor thing, she is naturally worried about Lucy; and she tells me that her husband, that is, Lucy's father, had the same habit and would get up, dress, and go out at night if not stopped.</t1608> <t1609>Lucy will get married in the fall; she is already planning her clothes and how her house should be arranged.</t1609> <t1610>I understand her because I am doing the same, only Jonathan and I will start life from a much simpler place and try to make ends meet.</t1610> <t1611>Mr. Holmwood - that is, the Honorable Arthur Holmwood, the only son of Lord Godalming - is coming here very soon, as soon as he manages to leave town, because his father is not very well; I think dear Lucy is counting the seconds until he arrives.</t1611> <t1612>She wants to take him to the bench on the hill in the churchyard and show him the beauty of Whitby.</t1612> <t1613>I can tell that what bothers her is the waiting; she will be fine when Mr. Holmwood arrives.</t1613>
<t1614>July 27.</t1614> <t1615>- No news from Jonathan.</t1615> <t1616>Although I don't know why I should be, I am getting increasingly worried about him; I keep thinking that I wish he would write something, even if it's just a single line.</t1616> <t1617>Lucy is walking more than ever and I wake up every night to her movements in the room.</t1617> <t1618>Fortunately, the weather is so hot that it's impossible for her to catch a cold; but still, the anxiety I feel and the constant waking up are affecting me badly; I am nervous and sleepless.</t1618> <t1619>Thank God, Lucy's health is good.</t1619> <t1620>Mr. Holmwood was suddenly called to Ring to see his father, who has become seriously ill.</t1620> <t1621>Lucy is sad about the postponement of seeing him, but this situation does not affect her appearance; she has gained a little weight and her cheeks have turned a beautiful rosy pink.</t1621>
<t1622>There is no trace left of the previous pale appearance.</t1622> <t1623>I pray that it continues this way.</t1623>
<t1624>August 3rd.</t1624> <t1625>- Another week has passed and still no news from Jonathan, even mr Hawkins, who writes to me, hasn't heard anything.</t1625> <t1626>Oh! I hope he is not ill.</t1626> <t1627>I am looking at his last letter, but somehow it doesn't satisfy me.</t1627> <t1628>It's not his style at all, but it's his handwriting.</t1628> <t1629>There is no doubt about that.</t1629> <t1630>In the past week, Lucy hasn't walked much in her sleep, but lately, there is a strange intensity about her; she seems to be watching me even in her sleep.</t1630> <t1631>She tries the door and when she sees it's locked, she wanders around the room looking for the key.</t1631>
<t1632>August 6.</t1632> <t1633>- Three more days have passed, no news.</t1633> <t1634>This uncertainty has started to become terrifying.</t1634> <t1635>If I knew where to write or where to go, I would feel more at ease; but since that last letter, no one has heard a single word from Jonathan.</t1635> <t1636>I must pray to God to give me patience.</t1636> <t1637>Lucy gets excited more easily than ever, but other than that, she is fine.</t1637> <t1638>Last night was very frightening, and the fishermen said that a storm was inevitable.</t1638> <t1639>I must watch and learn the signs in the air.</t1639> <t1640>Today is a gray day, and as I write these, the sun is hidden behind thick clouds over Kettleness.</t1640> <t1641>Everything is gray - except for the green grass that looks like emerald among the grays - gray earthy rock; gray clouds leaning over the gray sea where the sand dunes stretch out like gray fingers, slightly colored by the sunlight in the distance.</t1641> <t1642>The sea falls over the beach and sandy areas, choking and roaring as it accumulates inland.</t1642> <t1643>The horizon has disappeared into a gray mist.</t1643> <t1644>Everything is vast; the clouds are piled up like gigantic rocks, and there is a sound over the sea that comes like a sign of doom to the ears.</t1644> <t1645>On the beach, here and there, there are dark shapes sometimes half-hidden in the mists, giving the impression of "trees walking like men."</t1645> <t1646>The fishing boats are racing to return home, bobbing up and down in the ground swell as they drift towards the harbor, tilting from side to side as if about to sink.</t1646> <t1647>Here comes old Mr. Swales.</t1647> <t1648>He is coming directly towards me, and from the way he lifts his hat, I understand he wants to talk...</t1648>
<t1649>I was quite affected by the change in the poor old man.</t1649> <t1650>When he sat next to me, he said very kindly:</t1650>
<t1651>"I want to tell you something, young lady."</t1651> <t1652>I could see that she didn't feel comfortable, so I took her poor old wrinkled hand in mine and asked her to explain everything in detail; thus, holding her hand in mine, she said:</t1652>
<t1653>“I fear, my child, that I must have shaken you with the bad things I said about the dead and similar matters last week; but I didn't actually mean it that way, and I want you to remember this when I am gone.</t1653> <t1654>We, the cracked old folks with one foot in the grave, do not want to think about this matter at all and fear it; that is why I make light of it, so that I can ease my heart a little.</t1654> <t1655>But God bless you, little lady, I am not afraid of dying, not at all; only, if I could help it, I wouldn't want to die.</t1655> <t1656>My time must be approaching now, because I am old and a hundred years is far more than one can expect; I am so close to death that the Old Man has already started sharpening his scythe.</t1656> <t1657>You see, I can't break the habit of joking all at once.</t1657> <t1658>Soon, one day, the angel of death will come for me.</t1658> <t1659>But don't be sad, my child,” because he had seen me crying - “if he were to come tonight, I would not refuse to answer his call.</t1659> <t1660>Because life is just waiting for something other than what we do, and the only thing we can truly rely on is death.</t1660> <t1661>But I am content with this because it is coming for me, and it is coming swiftly, my child.</t1661> <t1662>It may come while we are looking and wondering.</t1662> <t1663>Perhaps, it is coming with the wind over the sea, bringing along lost things, shipwrecks, tension, and sad hearts.</t1663> <t1664>Look! Look!” he suddenly shouted.</t1664> <t1665>“There is something in that wind that looks like, tastes like, and smells like death.</t1665> <t1666>In the air; I feel it coming.</t1666> <t1667>God, let me respond with joy when my time comes!” He raised his arms to the sky with all his heart and took off his hat.</t1667> <t1668>His mouth moved as if he were praying.</t1668> <t1669>After a few minutes of silence, he stood up, shook hands with me, blessed me, bid farewell, and limped away.</t1669> <t1670>All this touched me deeply and left me in turmoil.</t1670>
<t1671>I was pleased when the coast guard came to me with his small binoculars under his arm.</t1671> <t1672>As usual, he stopped to talk to me, but he kept looking at a strange ship.</t1672>
<t1673>“I couldn't make out what it was,” he said; “judging by its appearance, it looks like a Russian ship; but it's drifting in a very strange manner.</t1673> <t1674>It seems undecided about what to do; as if it sees the approaching storm but can't decide whether to head north, out to the open sea, or enter the harbor here.</t1674> <t1675>Look, there it goes again! It's moving very oddly because it doesn't heed the hand on the helm; it changes direction with every gust of wind.</t1675> <t1676>By this time tomorrow, we'll hear more about it.</t1676><t1677>”</t1677>
<t1678>VII</t1678>
<t1679>Clipping from the Dailygraph, August 8</t1679>
<t1680>(Pasted into Mina Murray's journal.</t1680><t1681>)</t1681>
<t1682>From a reporter</t1682>
<t1683>Whitby</t1683>
<t1684>With its strange and unique results, the largest and most sudden storm on record occurred here.</t1684> <t1685>The weather was quite hot and humid, but it was not unusual for the month of August.</t1685> <t1686>Saturday evening was one of the best-known days, and the holiday crowd had planned visits to Mulgrave Woods, Robin Hood's Bay, Rig Mill, Runswick, Staithes, and various trips to places near Whitby for yesterday.</t1686> <t1687>The steamships named Emma and Scarborough were making excursion trips up and down the coastline, and there was an unusual number of "tour trips" coming to and departing from Whitby.</t1687> <t1688>In the afternoon, some regulars who often visited the East Cliff Churchyard and were accustomed to watching the vast sea stretching to the north and east from the first-class viewpoint noted the sudden appearance of "mare's tails" in the northwest, until then the day had been unusually beautiful.</t1688> <t1689>At that time, the wind was blowing lightly from the southwest, which in barometer terms is classified as a "force two light breeze."</t1689> <t1690>The coastguard on duty immediately prepared a report, and an old fisherman who had been observing the weather indicators at East Cliff for over half a century emphatically announced that an unexpected storm was about to break.</t1690> <t1691>The arrival of sunset was so beautiful, so majestic among the gloriously colored cloud formations, that a crowd had gathered on the cliffside path of the old churchyard to enjoy this beauty.</t1691> <t1692>Before the sun set beneath the landmass of Kettleness, as it boldly stood against the western sky, the descending path was painted with endless colors - every shade of fiery red, purple, pink, green, violet, and gold; alongside huge shadows here and there, all kinds of small but visible land shapes could be seen.</t1692> <t1693>The experience did not fail to impress the painters, and some sketches of the "Great Storm Before" will undoubtedly honor the walls of the Royal Academy and the Royal Institute next May.</t1693> <t1694>More than one captain immediately decided that the "cobble" or "cutter" (as they called different classes of ships) should stay in the harbor until the storm subsided.</t1694> <t1695>By evening, the wind had completely died down, and by midnight, the oppressive heat and the intense silence that precedes a storm, affecting sensitive people, had set in.</t1695> <t1696>At sea, there were very few lights visible because even the steamships that "hugged" the coast closely had moved far out, and only a few fishing boats were in sight.</t1696> <t1697>The only visible sailing ship appeared to be a foreign schooner heading west with all its sails set.</t1697> <t1698>During the time it remained in view, the recklessness and ignorance of the sailors on board became a fruitful topic of commentary, and efforts were made to signal it to lower its sails in the face of danger.</t1698> <t1699>Before night fully set in, it was seen gently rising and falling on the waves of the sea, its sails flapping idly:</t1699>
<t1700>In a painted sea, painted</t1700>
<t1701>adrift like a ship.</t1701> <t1702>18</t1702>
<t1703>Shortly before ten o'clock, the calmness of the air became quite oppressive; the silence was so pronounced that the bleating of a lamb inland and the barking of a dog in the town could be clearly heard, and the lively French melody played by the band on the quay clashed with the great harmony created by nature's silence.</t1703> <t1704>Shortly after midnight, a strange sound came from the sea, and the air carried a peculiar, indistinct, and deep rumbling from above.</t1704>
<t1705>Then, suddenly, a storm broke out.</t1705><t1706>At that moment, it was at an unimaginable speed, even impossible to comprehend afterward; suddenly, nature had begun to shake entirely.</t1706><t1707>The waves were rising with increasing fury, each one surpassing the previous one; until, in just a few minutes, the sea that had looked like glass turned into a roaring and all-consuming monster.</t1707><t1708>The white-capped waves were madly pounding the sands at sea level and rising towards the sloping cliffs; others were crashing into the pier and washing the lighthouses at the ends of each of the Whitby Harbor piers with their foam.</t1708><t1709>The wind was roaring like a storm and blowing with such force that even strong men could barely stand or had to hold onto iron poles tightly.</t1709><t1710>It became clear that it was necessary to clear the piers of the crowds of spectators, otherwise the deadly events of the night would multiply.</t1710><t1711>In addition to the difficulties and dangers of the moment, masses of fog from the sea were drifting inland - ghostly white, wet clouds; so wet, damp, and cold that it didn't take much imagination to think that the souls lost at sea were touching their friends with the cold hand of death; many shivered as the sea fog rolled in waves.</t1711><t1712>From time to time, the fog would clear, and under the light of lightning followed by sudden thunderclaps that made the sky above us seem to tremble in astonishment at the storm's steps, the sea could be seen from afar.</t1712><t1713>Therefore, part of the emerging scene was immeasurably magnificent and interesting - the sea rising like a mountain, throwing masses of white foam skyward with each wave, and the storm seemed to be carrying them into space; here and there, a fishing boat with tattered sails was frantically hurrying to find shelter before the storm fully broke; occasionally, the white wings of a sea bird thrown by the storm could be seen.</t1713><t1714>The new beacon at the top of East Cliff was ready for the experiment, but it had never been tested before.</t1714><t1715>The officials responsible for it got to work and scanned the sea surface with the beacon during the intervals given by the fog rushing inland.</t1715><t1716>Once or twice, it worked very well; for instance, a fishing boat with its bulwark underwater managed to quickly enter the harbor, avoiding the danger of crashing into the piers thanks to its guiding light.</t1716><t1717>With each boat reaching the safety of the harbor, a cheer of joy rose from the crowd on the shore; this cheer momentarily interrupted the storm and then seemed to be lifted off the ground within its speed.</t1717><t1718>Soon, the beacon spotted a schooner with all sails set ahead; it was evidently the boat noticed earlier in the evening.</t1718><t1719>By this time, the wind had turned east, and a shudder ran through the spectators on the cliffs as they realized the terrible danger the boat was now in.</t1719><t1720>Between the ship and the harbor lay a flat rock where many good ships had struggled from time to time, and with the current wind direction, it seemed almost impossible for the ship to find the harbor entrance.</t1720><t1721>It was almost time for the tide to rise, but the waves were so large that the shallow areas along the shore were nearly visible in the troughs between them; the schooner with all sails set was advancing at such a speed that, as the old sailors said, "Even if it were Hell, it was certain to reach somewhere."</t1721><t1722>Then came a wave of sea fog larger than any seen so far - a damp and cold mass of fog covering everything like a gray shroud and leaving people with only their sense of hearing; only their sense of hearing remained because the roar of the storm, the rumble of thunder, and the roar of the great waves came through the damp nothingness even stronger than before.</t1722><t1723>The beacon's rays were fixed on the harbor mouth at the East Pier where the collision was expected, and the men held their breath and waited.</t1723><t1724>The wind suddenly turned northeast, and the remnants of the sea fog melted away with the breeze; then, mirabile dictu, between the piers, a strange schooner with all sails set reached the safety of the harbor at breakneck speed before the collision.</t1724><t1725>The beacon followed it, and everyone who saw the ship was horrified because a corpse with its head bowed was tied to the helm, and with each movement of the ship, the head of the corpse was swinging back and forth in a ghastly manner.</t1725><t1726>No one else was visible on deck.</t1726><t1727>When they realized that the ship had miraculously found the harbor guided only by the hand of a dead man, a great fear fell upon everyone!</t1727><t1728>However, all this happened in a much shorter time than it takes to write these words.</t1728><t1729>The schooner did not stop and quickly advanced in the harbor, grounding on the sand and gravel pile known to the locals as Tate Hill Pier, which jutted out under East Cliff, washed by many tides and storms.</t1729>
<t1730>Of course, when the ship reached the sandbank, there was a great jolt.</t1730> <t1731>Every mast, every rope, every stay was strained, and part of the cargo on the deck spilled onto the ground.</t1731> <t1732>But the strangest thing was that as soon as the ship touched the shore, a huge dog appeared on the lower deck as if it had been thrown by the impact and leapt from the ship's bow onto the sand.</t1732> <t1733>Directly, it headed towards the steep hill where the soil from the top had fallen down, exposing some gravestones - known locally in Whitby as "yard stones" or "long stones" due to their horizontal position caused by the steepness - disappearing into the darkness that seemed to concentrate just beyond the focus of the lighthouse.</t1733>
<t1734>By chance, there was no one at Tate Hill Wharf at that time because everyone who lived nearby was either in their beds or up on the hills.</t1734> <t1735>Therefore, the coast guard on duty on the east side of the harbor, who ran to the small wharf below, was the first person to board the ship.</t1735> <t1736>After the men operating the searchlight scanned the harbor entrance without seeing anything, they turned the light towards the shipwreck and kept it there.</t1736> <t1737>The coast guard ran to the stern of the ship, and when he reached the helm, he bent down to examine it and immediately pulled back as if struck by a sudden emotion.</t1737> <t1738>This situation aroused public curiosity, and a large number of people began to run.</t1738> <t1739>West Cliff is quite a distance from Tate Hill Wharf via the suspension bridge, but your reporter is a fairly good runner and reached the ship before the crowd.</t1739> <t1740>However, when I arrived, I saw that a crowd had already gathered on the wharf, and the coast guard and police were not allowing them to board the ship.</t1740> <t1741>With the permission of the chief sailor, I was allowed to board the deck as your reporter and joined the small group who saw the dead sailor tied to the helm.</t1741>
<t1742>The coast guard officer being surprised, even scared, is not for nothing because such a scene is rarely seen.</t1742> <t1743>The man's hands were tied first to each other and then to a stick of the helm.</t1743> <t1744>There was a cross between his palm and the wood, and the rosary attached to the cross was wrapped around both his wrists and the helm, all supported by strings.</t1744> <t1745>Perhaps the poor man was sitting at first, but as the sails flapped and struggled with the wind, the helm moved his body from its place, causing him to be dragged back and forth; so much so that the strings he was tied with cut into his flesh down to the bone.</t1745> <t1746>A definitive report on the incident was written, and the doctor who came immediately after me - Surgeon J.</t1746> <t1747>M.</t1747> <t1748>Caffyn from 33 East Elliot Place - stated after the examination that the man must have been dead for at least two days.</t1748> <t1749>In his pocket was a carefully corked bottle; inside it was nothing but a small roll of paper that seemed to be an addition to the logbook.</t1749> <t1750>The coast guard officer said that the man must have tied his own hands by undoing the knots with his teeth.</t1750> <t1751>The fact that the coast guard officer was the first to board the ship could prevent some difficulties that might arise in the Maritime Court in the future, because the coast guard officer cannot claim the right to salvaged goods granted to the first civilian to board a shipwreck.</t1751> <t1752>However, the legal jaws had already dropped, and a young law student loudly argued that ownership rights had already been lost, that it was held in violation of unsellable property legislation because although the helm's tiller was not proof of power of attorney, it was symbolically held in a dead hand.</t1752> <t1753>Needless to say, the dead helmsman, who kept his honorable watch until his death - showing a patience as noble as that of the young "Casabianca" - was taken from his place with great respect and brought to the morgue to await the official investigation.</t1753>
<t1754>The storm that suddenly broke out is already starting to pass and its intensity is decreasing; the crowds are dispersing to their homes and the sky is beginning to redden over the Yorkshire moors.</t1754> <t1755>For your next issue, I will send more details about the abandoned ship that miraculously found its way to the harbor during the storm.</t1755>
<t1756>Whitby</t1756>
<t1757>August 9.</t1757><t1758>- The events following the strange arrival of the abandoned ship in last night's storm are almost more frightening than the event itself.</t1758><t1759>The ship turned out to be a Russian vessel from Varna, named Demeter.</t1759><t1760>The ship is almost entirely filled with silver sand; it has very little cargo - a few large wooden crates filled with soil.</t1760><t1761>This cargo was received this morning by Mr. S., a legal advisor from Whitby, who officially took delivery of the goods sent to him.</t1761><t1762>F.</t1762><t1763>Billington, sent to number 7 Crescent.</t1763><t1764>The Russian consul also officially took delivery of the ship under the charter agreement and paid all port fees, etc.</t1764><t1765>Nothing else is being talked about here today other than this strange coincidence; the Chamber of Commerce officials were very meticulous about ensuring everything was done according to current regulations.</t1765><t1766>They are clearly determined that there should be no reason for complaints later, as people's interest will soon wane.</t1766><t1767>The dog that landed when the ship struck was a great curiosity, and more than one member of the SPCA, which is very strong in Whitby, rushed to embrace the animal.</t1767><t1768>However, the dog's disappearance caused general disappointment; it seemed to have vanished from the town entirely.</t1768><t1769>Perhaps it got scared and headed for the bushes; it might still be hiding there in fear.</t1769><t1770>There are those who look at such a possibility with fear, thinking it might be a danger in the future, as it is clearly a wild animal.</t1770><t1771>Early this morning, a large shepherd dog mix belonging to a coal merchant near Tate Hill Pier was found dead on the road opposite its owner's yard.</t1771><t1772>It had fought and clearly had a wild opponent, as its throat was torn, and its abdomen was slashed open as if attacked by a predatory claw.</t1772>
<t1773>Later.</t1773> <t1774>- Thanks to the courtesy of the Chamber of Commerce inspector, I was allowed to glance at the logbook of the Demeter, which was kept regularly until the last three days but contained no remarkable data other than the facts about the missing men.</t1774> <t1775>However, the truly interesting thing was the paper that came out of the bottle and was presented at the official inquiry today; I have never encountered a narrative stranger than what these two documents combined create.</t1775> <t1776>Since there is no reason to conceal them, I was permitted to use them; I am sending you a copy of the technical details related to shipping and cargo transport.</t1776> <t1777>It almost seems that the captain succumbed to some kind of madness before dying in the blue waters of the sea, and this condition appears to have increased throughout the voyage.</t1777> <t1778>Of course, my statement should be taken cum grano23 because, due to the short time, I am writing this as dictated by the Russian consulate clerk who kindly translated them for me.</t1778>
<t1779>Demeter's logbook</t1779>
<t1780>From Varna to Whitby</t1780>
<t1781>It was written on July 18th, such strange things are happening that I will record everything in detail until we reach the shore.</t1781>
<t1782>On July 6, we completed loading our cargo of chests filled with silver sand and soil.</t1782> <t1783>At noon, we set sail.</t1783> <t1784>The east wind, fresh.</t1784> <t1785>The crew, five deckhands, ... two deck officers, a cook, and myself (the captain).</t1785>
<t1786>At dawn on July 11, we entered the Bosphorus Strait in Istanbul.</t1786> <t1787>Turkish customs officers boarded the ship.</t1787> <t1788>Tip.</t1788> <t1789>Everything is settled.</t1789> <t1790>We set off at 4 in the evening.</t1790>
<t1791>We passed through the Dardanelles on July 12.</t1791> <t1792>More customs officers and the flagship of the security fleet.</t1792> <t1793>Again, a tip.</t1793> <t1794>The officers worked meticulously, but the job was done quickly.</t1794> <t1795>They want us to leave immediately.</t1795> <t1796>We passed through the archipelago in the dark.</t1796>
<t1797>We passed Cape Matapan on July 13th.</t1797> <t1798>The crew is discontent about something.</t1798> <t1799>They seem scared, but they are not saying it outright.</t1799>
<t1800>On July 14, I had concerns regarding the crew.</t1800> <t1801>They are all sturdy men who have sailed with me before.</t1801> <t1802>The deck officer couldn't understand what the issue was; they just told him there was something on the ship and made the sign of the cross.</t1802> <t1803>That day, the deck officer got very angry and hit someone.</t1803> <t1804>I expected a heated fight, but everything was calm.</t1804>
<t1805>On the morning of July 16, the deck officer reported that one of the crew members, Petrovski, was missing.</t1805> <t1806>He couldn't provide an explanation.</t1806> <t1807>The night before, he had taken the port watch at eight; he handed over the watch to Abramov, but did not return to his bunk.</t1807> <t1808>The men are more cheerless than ever.</t1808> <t1809>They all said they expected something like this, but they weren't saying more than that there was something on the ship.</t1809> <t1810>The deck officer is getting increasingly nervous; I fear there might be trouble ahead.</t1810>
<t1811>One day before July 17, one of the men, Olgaren, came to my cabin and explained in fear that he believed there was a stranger on the ship.</t1811> <t1812>During his watch, when he took shelter behind the wheelhouse because of a rainstorm, he saw a tall, thin man who did not resemble any of the crew members come out from the deck doorway and disappear as he walked along the deck.</t1812> <t1813>He had carefully watched the man and found no one when he reached the bow of the ship; all the hatchways were closed.</t1813> <t1814>He was seized by a fear based on superstition, and I am afraid this panic might spread.</t1814> <t1815>To quell it, I will carefully search the entire ship from top to bottom today.</t1815>
<t1816>Later in the day, I gathered the entire crew and told them that since they thought someone was on board, we would search every inch of the ship.</t1816> <t1817>The first deck officer was angry; he said it was nonsense and that giving in to such foolish thoughts would lower the men's morale, and he promised to keep them out of trouble with a stick.</t1817> <t1818>When the others started searching carefully side by side with lanterns in hand, I left the helm to the deck officer; we did not leave a single corner unsearched.</t1818> <t1819>There were no odd corners where a man could hide, just large wooden crates.</t1819> <t1820>When the search ended, the men were greatly relieved and returned to work cheerfully.</t1820> <t1821>The first deck officer frowned but said nothing.</t1821>
<t1822>July 22.</t1822> <t1823>- The weather has been stormy for the last three days and the entire crew is busy with the sails - no time to be afraid.</t1823> <t1824>The men seem to have forgotten their fears.</t1824> <t1825>The deck officer has regained his cheerfulness and everyone is getting along well with each other.</t1825> <t1826>I praised the men for their work in the bad weather.</t1826> <t1827>We passed through the Strait of Gibraltar.</t1827> <t1828>Everything is fine.</t1828>
<t1829>July 24.</t1829> <t1830>- It's as if there's a bad fate over the ship.</t1830> <t1831>We are already one crew member short, and with the bad weather, as we entered the Bay of Biscay, another man went missing - disappeared.</t1831> <t1832>Like the first one, he finished his watch and was never seen again.</t1832> <t1833>All the men are in a panic from fear; they wanted to keep watch in pairs because they are afraid to be alone.</t1833> <t1834>The deck officer got angry.</t1834> <t1835>I'm afraid there will be some trouble, because either he or the crew will resort to violence.</t1835>
<t1836>July 28.</t1836> <t1837>- Four days in a kind of vortex where we were battered and the wind turned into a storm in hell.</t1837> <t1838>No sleep for anyone.</t1838> <t1839>All the men are exhausted.</t1839> <t1840>I don't quite know how to organize the watches as no one has the strength to continue.</t1840> <t1841>The second deck officer volunteered to take the helm, keep watch, and allow the men to sleep for a few hours.</t1841> <t1842>The wind is dying down; the sea is still terrible, but we feel it less as the ship is more stable.</t1842>
<t1843>July 29.</t1843> <t1844>- Another disaster.</t1844> <t1845>Since the crew was too tired to keep watch in pairs, single watches were held tonight.</t1845> <t1846>When the morning watchman came to the deck, he saw no one around except the helmsman.</t1846> <t1847>He shouted, and everyone came to the deck.</t1847> <t1848>Everywhere was searched thoroughly, but no one was found.</t1848> <t1849>We no longer have our second deck officer, and the crew is in a panic.</t1849> <t1850>The deck officer and I have agreed to walk around armed from now on and wait for a signal.</t1850>
<t1851>July 30.</t1851> <t1852>- Last night.</t1852> <t1853>I was happy because we were approaching England.</t1853> <t1854>The weather is nice, all sails are open.</t1854> <t1855>I retreated to the cabin exhausted; I slept soundly; I was awakened by the deck officer who said that both the watchman and the helmsman were missing.</t1855> <t1856>Only the deck officer, two crew members, and I were left to navigate the ship.</t1856>
<t1857>August 1st.</t1857> <t1858>- There has been fog for two days and no ship has been seen.</t1858> <t1859>I had hoped to send a distress signal or to enter somewhere when we entered the English Channel.</t1859> <t1860>Since we do not have the strength to use the sails, we must take the wind at our back.</t1860> <t1861>We do not dare to lower the sails because we would not be able to raise them again.</t1861> <t1862>It felt like we were drifting towards a terrible end, caught in the current.</t1862> <t1863>The deck officer's morale was worse than that of the other two men.</t1863> <t1864>His strong nature seemed to be working against him internally.</t1864> <t1865>The men are worse than scared; they have prepared their minds for the worst, working insensibly and patiently.</t1865> <t1866>The men are Russian, the deck officer is Romanian.</t1866>
<t1867>2</t1867> <t1868>August, midnight.</t1868> <t1869>- I woke up from a few minutes of sleep, apparently due to a scream coming from outside my door.</t1869> <t1870>I couldn't see anything in the fog.</t1870> <t1871>I ran to the deck and encountered the deck officer.</t1871> <t1872>He said he heard the scream and ran, but there was no sign of the man on watch.</t1872> <t1873>Another one is gone.</t1873> <t1874>God help us!</t1874> <t1875>The deck officer says we must have passed the Dover Strait because the fog lifted for a moment just as he heard the man scream, and he saw the North Foreland.</t1875> <t1876>If so, it means we have now entered the North Sea, and only God can guide us in this fog that seems to move with us; yet it seems God has forsaken us.</t1876>
<t1877>August 3rd.</t1877> <t1878>- At midnight, I went to relieve the man at the helm, but when I arrived, I found no one there.</t1878> <t1879>The wind had calmed, and since we were sailing before the wind, we were not off course.</t1879> <t1880>I didn't dare leave the helm, so I called out to the deck officer.</t1880> <t1881>Within seconds, he rushed to the deck in his undershirt.</t1881> <t1882>He looked at me with wild eyes and appeared exhausted; I feared he had completely lost his mind.</t1882> <t1883>He came close to me and, as if afraid the air might hear us, he whispered hoarsely into my ear: "He's here; now I know.</t1883> <t1884>Last night, while on watch, I saw that Thing; it looked human, tall, thin, and deathly pale.</t1884> <t1885>It was standing at the bow, looking out.</t1885> <t1886>I sneaked up behind it and stabbed that Thing with my knife; but the knife passed through it as if it were air."</t1886> <t1887>And as he spoke, he drew out his knife and stabbed it fiercely into the air.</t1887> <t1888>Then he continued: "But that Thing is here, and I will find it.</t1888> <t1889>Maybe it's hiding inside one of those crates.</t1889> <t1890>I will pry the nails off each one and see.</t1890> <t1891>You hold the helm."</t1891> <t1892>He went below with a warning look and his finger to his lips.</t1892> <t1893>The wind was shifting, and I couldn't leave the helm.</t1893> <t1894>I saw him come back on deck with a tool chest and a lantern, then descend through the forward hatch.</t1894> <t1895>He's gone mad, completely deranged; there's no point in trying to stop him.</t1895> <t1896>He can't harm those big crates: they were billed as "Mud," the most harmless thing he could do is push them around.</t1896> <t1897>So, I stay here at the helm, writing this.</t1897> <t1898>I can only trust in God and wait for the fog to lift.</t1898> <t1899>Then, if I can't steer to a port with the wind, I'll cut the sails and signal for help...</t1899>
<t1900>Almost everything is now over.</t1900> <t1901>Just as I was beginning to hope that the deck officer would appear more composed - because I had heard him hitting something in the hold, and working would do him good - a blood-curdling, unexpected, and fear-filled scream came from the hatch; the deck officer shot up to the deck like a bullet - a furious madman with eyes wild and face contorted with fear.</t1901> <t1902>"Save me!</t1902> <t1903>Save me!" he shouted; he looked around at his surroundings shrouded in mist.</t1903> <t1904>His fear turned to despair, and in a calm voice, he said, "You'd better come too, captain, before it's too late.</t1904> <t1905>He's here."</t1905> <t1906>Now I know the secret.</t1906> <t1907>The sea will protect me from Him, and that's all that's left.</t1907> <t1908>Before I could say a single word or move to stop him, he dashed to the rail and deliberately threw himself into the sea.</t1908> <t1909>I think now I know the secret too.</t1909> <t1910>This madman who had gotten rid of the men one by one, and now he followed them.</t1910> <t1911>God, help me!</t1911> <t1912>How will I account for all these horrifying events when I reach the port?</t1912> <t1913>When I reach the port!</t1913> <t1914>Will such a thing even happen?</t1914>
<t1915>August 4.</t1915> <t1916>- The fog that even the sunrise cannot pierce still persists.</t1916> <t1917>I know the sun has risen because I am a sailor, for no other reason do I know.</t1917> <t1918>I did not dare to go below, I did not dare to leave the helm; so I stayed here all night and in the dimness of the night I saw that Thing - that Man!</t1918> <t1919>God forgive me, but the deck officer was right to jump off the ship.</t1919> <t1920>It is better to die like a human; no one can object to dying like a sailor in the blue waters.</t1920> <t1921>But I am the captain and I must not abandon my ship.</t1921> <t1922>But I will outwit this enemy or monster, because when my strength begins to wane, I will tie my hands to the helm and with them I will tie something that this Man - this Thing! - dares not touch; then, whether the wind be good or bad, I will preserve my soul and honor like a captain.</t1922> <t1923>I am weakening and night is falling.</t1923> <t1924>If he can fix his gaze on me once more, I may not have time to act.</t1924> <t1925>If we have an accident, perhaps this bottle may be found and those who find it may understand what happened; otherwise, well, let everyone know that I remained faithful to what was entrusted to me.</t1925> <t1926>God, the Holy Virgin, and the saints help the poor, ignorant soul trying to fulfill his duty...</t1926>
<t1927>Of course, the outcome of the matter was open to interpretation.</t1927> <t1928>There is no evidence at all; and there is no one to say whether this man committed the murders.</t1928> <t1929>Almost the entire public argues that the captain is a hero, and therefore a public funeral will be held for him.</t1929> <t1930>It has already been decided that his body will be taken to Esk with a boat convoy and then brought back to Tate Hill Pier and carried up the monastery steps; because he will be buried in the churchyard on the hillside.</t1930> <t1931>More than a hundred boat owners have already given their names, stating that they want to follow the captain to his grave.</t1931>
<t1932>There is no trace of the big dog; this is causing much sorrow because, considering the current state of public opinion, I believe the dog would have been adopted by the town.</t1932> <t1933>The funeral will be held tomorrow; thus, another "sea mystery" will come to an end.</t1933>
<t1934>Mina Murray's journal</t1934>
<t1935>August 8.</t1935> <t1936>- Lucy was very restless all night, so I couldn't sleep either.</t1936> <t1937>The storm was frightening, and the wind made a loud clattering noise in the chimney caps, causing me to startle.</t1937> <t1938>When a strong and sudden gust blew, the sound was like a gunshot in the distance.</t1938> <t1939>It's strange that Lucy didn't wake up; but she got up and dressed twice.</t1939> <t1940>Fortunately, each time I woke up in time and managed to undress her and put her back to bed without waking her.</t1940> <t1941>This sleepwalking issue is very peculiar, because as soon as her will is obstructed by any physical means, her purpose - if there is one - disappears, and she completely surrenders to the ordinary course of her life.</t1941>
<t1942>In the morning, we both got up early and went to the harbor to see if anything had happened during the night.</t1942> <t1943>There were very few people around; despite the sun being bright and the air clear and clean, the waves, which looked dark because of the foam on their crests like snow, were forcing their way through the narrow mouth of the harbor like a bully pushing through a crowd.</t1943> <t1944>For some reason, I was glad that Jonathan was on land rather than at sea last night.</t1944> <t1945>But, oh, on land or at sea?</t1945> <t1946>Where and how?</t1946> <t1947>I am worried for him in fear.</t1947> <t1948>I wish I knew what to do and could do something!</t1948>
<t1949>August 10.</t1949><t1950>- The poor captain's funeral today was very sad.</t1950><t1951>It seemed like all the boats in the harbor were there, and the coffin was carried by the captains from Tate Hill Pier all the way to the churchyard.</t1951><t1952>Lucy came with me, and we went early to our old bench and sat down as the boat procession went up the viaduct and then back down.</t1952><t1953>We had a very good view and watched the procession almost from start to finish.</t1953><t1954>The poor man was buried very close to our bench; thus, when the time came, we stood on the bench and saw everything.</t1954><t1955>Poor Lucy looked very sad.</t1955><t1956>She was constantly restless and tense; I couldn't help but think that the dreams she had at night were having a bad effect on her.</t1956><t1957>She is acting quite strangely about one thing: She doesn't admit that there is any reason for her restlessness, or if there is such a reason, she doesn't understand it herself.</t1957><t1958>There is another reason for her restlessness: Poor old Mr. Swales was found dead on our bench this morning with his neck broken.</t1958><t1959>According to the doctor, he clearly fell onto the bench out of some kind of terror, because his face had an expression of fear and horror that, according to the men, made their hair stand on end.</t1959><t1960>Poor dear old man!</t1960><t1961>Perhaps his dying eyes saw Death!</t1961><t1962>Lucy is so sweet and sensitive that she feels such effects much more strongly than other people.</t1962><t1963>Now she is very upset over a small matter that I, personally, don't care much about despite liking animals a lot.</t1963><t1964>One of the men who often comes here to look at the boats always has his dog with him.</t1964><t1965>The dog is always with him.</t1965><t1966>Both are very quiet beings; I have never seen the man get angry or the dog bark.</t1966><t1967>During the ceremony, the dog did not come to its owner, who was sitting on the bench next to us, but stood a few meters away, barking and howling.</t1967><t1968>The owner spoke to it first gently, then sternly, and finally angrily; but the dog neither came to him nor stopped making noise.</t1968><t1969>It was in some kind of rage; its eyes wild, its fur standing on end like the tail of a cat going into battle.</t1969><t1970>Eventually, the man also got angry, walked towards the dog, kicked it, and half-dragged, half-threw it onto the gravestone to which the bench was fixed.</t1970><t1971>As soon as it fell onto the stone, the poor thing went quiet and started trembling.</t1971><t1972>It didn't try to escape, it cowered and trembled in such a pitiable terror that I tried to soothe it, though it had little effect.</t1972><t1973>Lucy also felt very sorry for it, but she did not try to touch the dog, only looked at it with pained eyes.</t1973><t1974>I fear that Lucy's nature is too sensitive to go through life without trouble.</t1974><t1975>I am sure she will see this in her dreams tonight.</t1975><t1976>The combination of all these things - the ship being brought into the harbor by a dead man; the man being tied to the helm with a cross and rosary; the touching funeral ceremony; the dog in a state of furious terror - will provide material for her dreams.</t1976>
<t1977>I think the best thing will be for him to get physically tired and go to bed, so I will take him for a long walk on the slopes leading to Robin Hood's Bay and bring him back.</t1977> <t1978>Then, he won't have much desire for sleepwalking.</t1978>
<t1979>VIII</t1979>
<t1980>Mina Murray's journal</t1980>
<t1981>The same day, 11 PM.</t1981><t1982>- Ah! How tired I am!</t1982><t1983>If I hadn't made it a duty to keep a journal, I wouldn't open it tonight.</t1983><t1984>We had a very nice walk.</t1984><t1985>After a while, Lucy cheered up; I think we owe this to a few cute cows that slowly approached us in the field near the lighthouse and scared us.</t1985><t1986>I think we forgot everything except our personal fears, and in this way, we wiped the slate clean and made a fresh start.</t1986><t1987>At Robin Hood's Bay, we had a perfect "bitter tea" in a lovely, tiny, old-fashioned inn with a curved window right above the seaweed-covered rocks on the shore.</t1987><t1988>I'm sure our appetite would astonish the "New Woman".</t1988><t1989>Men are more tolerant, God bless them!</t1989><t1990>Then we walked home, taking a few, or rather many, rest breaks, with an unending fear of wild bulls in our hearts.</t1990><t1991>Lucy was really very tired, and we aimed to crawl into our beds as quickly as possible.</t1991><t1992>But the young preacher came to visit us, and Ms. Westenra asked him to stay for dinner.</t1992><t1993>Both Lucy and I fought the sleep fairy for him; I know it was a tough battle for me, but I acted quite heroically.</t1993><t1994>I think one day bishops should come together and find a way to produce a new class of preachers who, no matter how much they are insisted upon, do not stay for dinner and understand that the girls are tired.</t1994><t1995>Lucy fell asleep and is breathing lightly.</t1995><t1996>Her cheeks are more colorful than ever and oh, she looks so sweet.</t1996><t1997>If Mr. Holmwood fell in love with her just by seeing her in the living room, I wonder what he would say if he saw her now.</t1997><t1998>Someday, some "New Woman" writers will support the idea that men and women should be allowed to see each other while sleeping before proposing or accepting a marriage proposal.</t1998><t1999>But I think in the future, the "New Woman" will not show the humility to accept a marriage proposal; she will make the proposal herself.</t1999><t2000>And she will do a very good job at it!</t2000><t2001>We can find some consolation in this.</t2001><t2002>I am very happy tonight because Lucy looks better.</t2002><t2003>Really, I believe she has overcome the danger and the problems related to dreaming.</t2003><t2004>My happiness would be complete if I knew Jonathan was well, God bless and protect him.</t2004>
<t2005>August 11, 3 AM.</t2005><t2006>- Diary again.</t2006><t2007>Now there's no sleep, so I might as well write.</t2007><t2008>I'm too excited to sleep.</t2008><t2009>We experienced such an adventure, such a surprising experience.</t2009><t2010>As soon as I closed my diary, I fell asleep.</t2010><t2011>I suddenly woke up, sitting up with a terrifying fear pressing down on me and a sense of emptiness around me.</t2011><t2012>The room was dark, so I couldn't see Lucy's bed; I quietly went over and searched for Lucy by touch.</t2012><t2013>The bed was empty.</t2013><t2014>I lit a match and saw that she wasn't in the room.</t2014><t2015>The door was closed, but it wasn't locked as I had left it.</t2015><t2016>I was afraid of waking her mother, who had been sicker than ever lately; so I put on some clothes and prepared to look for Lucy.</t2016><t2017>As I was leaving the room, it occurred to me that what she was wearing might give some clues about her purpose in sleepwalking.</t2017><t2018>A robe meant home; a dress meant outside.</t2018><t2019>Both the robe and the dress were in their places.</t2019><t2020>"Thank God!" I said to myself; "She can't have gone far, because she's only in her nightgown."</t2020><t2021>I ran downstairs and looked in the sitting room.</t2021><t2022>She wasn't there!</t2022><t2023>Then, with a growing fear that chilled my heart, I looked in all the other rooms with open doors in the house.</t2023><t2024>Finally, I reached the entrance door and saw that it was open.</t2024><t2025>It wasn't wide open, but the latch wasn't in place.</t2025><t2026>The household is quite diligent about locking the door every night, so I feared that Lucy had gone outside just as she was.</t2026><t2027>There was no time to think about what might have happened; an overwhelming, all-encompassing fear blurred all the details.</t2027><t2028>I grabbed a large, heavy shawl and ran outside.</t2028><t2029>When I reached the Crescent, the clock struck one, and there wasn't a single person in sight.</t2029><t2030>I ran along North Terrace, but there was no sign of the white figure I was expecting.</t2030><t2031>With hope or fear - I don't know which - of seeing Lucy on our favorite bench, I looked from the edge of North Cliff over the harbor to East Cliff on the other side.</t2031><t2032>There was a bright full moon and heavy, dark, moving clouds that turned the entire landscape into a visual feast of light and shadow as they drifted.</t2032><t2033>For a second or two, I couldn't see anything because a cloud's shadow darkened Saint Mary's Church and its surroundings.</t2033><t2034>Then, as the cloud passed, I saw the ruins of the Abbey reappear; a narrow line of light, as sharp as a sword cut, moved forward, and the church and churchyard began to come into view.</t2034><t2035>Whatever my expectation was, it wasn't in vain, because there, on our favorite bench, the moon's silver light was shining on a snow-white figure leaning back slightly.</t2035><t2036>The cloud quickly returned before I could see much more; the shadow almost immediately cut off the light, but it seemed to me that there was something dark standing behind the bench where the white figure had shone.</t2036><t2037>I couldn't tell what it was, whether human or animal; I didn't wait to look again; I ran down the steep steps leading to the pier and ran across the bridge, the only way to reach East Cliff from the fish market.</t2037><t2038>The town was dead, as I didn't see a single person; I was glad of this because I didn't want anyone to witness Lucy's condition.</t2038><t2039>Time and distance seemed infinite; my knees trembled, and I began to breathe heavily as I struggled up the endless stairs leading to the Abbey.</t2039><t2040>I must have gone quickly, but I felt as if lead weights were tied to my feet and all the joints in my body were rusted.</t2040><t2041>As I approached the top, I began to see the bench and the white figure, for I was now close enough to distinguish it despite the shadow's effect.</t2041><t2042>Undoubtedly, there was a long, black thing bending over the half-reclining white figure.</t2042><t2043>In horror, I shouted, "Lucy! Lucy!" and something lifted its head; from where I stood, I could see a white face with red, gleaming eyes.</t2043><t2044>Lucy didn't respond, and I ran to the entrance of the churchyard.</t2044><t2045>When I entered, the church was between me and the bench, and I lost sight of Lucy for about a minute.</t2045><t2046>When I began to see her again, the cloud had passed, and the moonlight was so bright that I could see Lucy lying slightly reclined with her head on the back of the bench.</t2046><t2047>She was completely alone, and there was no sign of any living thing around her.</t2047>
<t2048>When I leaned over, I could see that she was still asleep.</t2048> <t2049>Her lips were parted and she was breathing - not softly as usual, but with long, deep breaths as if she wanted to fill her lungs completely with each inhale.</t2049> <t2050>When I approached her, she raised her hand in her sleep and wrapped the collar of her nightgown around her neck.</t2050> <t2051>As she did this, she shivered slightly as if she felt the cold.</t2051> <t2052>I threw a warm shawl over her and fastened the edges tightly around her neck because I was afraid she might catch a bad chill in the night air while she was so exposed.</t2052> <t2053>I was afraid of waking her suddenly, so I secured the shawl around her neck with a large safety pin to free my hands and help her; but in my anxious clumsiness, I must have pricked or stuck her with the pin because soon after, as her breathing calmed, she brought her hand to her neck and groaned once more.</t2053> <t2054>Once I had carefully wrapped her up, I put my shoes on her feet and then began to wake her gently.</t2054> <t2055>At first, she did not respond, but she became increasingly restless, occasionally groaning and sighing in her sleep.</t2055> <t2056>Time was passing quickly, and for many other reasons, I wanted to get her home as soon as possible; <t2063>Luck was on our side and we reached home without encountering anyone.</t2063> <t2064>Once, as we were moving along the road ahead, we saw a man who was not very sober; but we hid in the recess of a doorway until the man disappeared down one of the steep paths called wynds in Scotland.</t2064> <t2065>My heart was beating so fast that I thought I would faint at times.</t2065> <t2066>I was very worried about Lucy; not only for her health, fearing she might be harmed by being exposed, but also for her reputation if the story were to spread from ear to ear.</t2066> <t2067>After we went inside, washed our feet, and said a prayer of thanks together, I put her to bed.</t2067> <t2068>Before falling asleep, she asked me not to tell anyone, not even her mother, about this sleepwalking adventure, she even begged for it.</t2068> <t2069>At first, I hesitated to promise this; but when I thought about her mother's health and how learning about this would worry her, and how such a story could be distorted if it leaked out -no, it would definitely be distorted- I decided it was wiser to do as she asked.</t2069> <t2070>I hope I did the right thing.</t2070> <t2071>I locked the door; the key is tied to my hand so that perhaps I won't be disturbed again.</t2071> <t2072>Lucy is sleeping soundly; the reflection of dawn can be seen over the sea...</t2072>
<t2073>Same day, noon.</t2073> <t2074>- Everything is fine.</t2074> <t2075>Lucy slept until I woke her up and she doesn't seem to have turned in her bed.</t2075> <t2076>The night's adventure doesn't seem to have harmed her; on the contrary, it seems to have benefited her, because this morning she looks better than she has in weeks.</t2076> <t2077>I'm sorry that my clumsiness with the safety pin hurt her.</t2077> <t2078>Indeed, the situation could have been serious because the skin on her neck is slightly pierced.</t2078> <t2079>I must have pierced a loose piece of skin because there are two small red dots on her neck like pinholes and a drop of blood on the collar band of her nightgown.</t2079> <t2080>When I apologized and said I was worried about it, she laughed, patted me, and said she didn't feel anything.</t2080> <t2081>Fortunately, since it is very tiny, it won't leave a mark.</t2081>

<t2091>August 12.</t2091> <t2092>- My expectations were wrong because I woke up twice during the night with Lucy trying to go outside.</t2092> <t2093>Even in her sleep, she seemed a bit impatient to find the door closed and returned to bed with a protesting attitude.</t2093> <t2094>I woke up at dawn and heard the chirping of birds in front of the window.</t2094> <t2095>Lucy also woke up, and I was glad to see that she was even better than the previous morning.</t2095> <t2096>Her old cheerful demeanor seemed to have completely returned; she came and curled up next to me and told me everything about Arthur; I told her how worried I was about Jonathan, and then she tried to comfort me.</t2096> <t2097>Well, she somehow managed to do so, because although empathy cannot change the facts, it can help make them more bearable.</t2097>
<t2098>August 13th.</t2098> <t2099>- Another calm day and, as on previous nights, I went to bed with the key tied to my hand.</t2099> <t2100>Once again, I woke up during the night and found Lucy sitting on the bed still asleep, pointing towards the window.</t2100> <t2101>I quietly got up and drew the curtain to look outside.</t2101> <t2102>There was a bright moonlight, and the soft effect of the light on the sea and sky - the two blending into each other in a great, silent mystery - was indescribably beautiful.</t2102> <t2103>A large bat was flying in wide circles between the moonlight and me.</t2103> <t2104>Once or twice it came quite close, but I think it got scared when it saw me, and flew away towards the Monastery, passing over the harbor.</t2104> <t2105>When I turned back from the window, Lucy had lain down again and was sleeping peacefully.</t2105> <t2106>She did not move at all for the rest of the night.</t2106>
<t2107>August 14.</t2107> <t2108>- I spent the whole day reading and writing at East Cliff.</t2108> <t2109>Lucy seems to love this place as much as I do, and it's hard to get her to leave and go home for lunch, tea, or dinner.</t2109> <t2110>She said something strange this afternoon.</t2110> <t2111>We were heading home for dinner and had reached the top of the stairs that start at the West Pier, and as usual, we stopped to look at the view.</t2111> <t2112>The sun, which had sunk quite low in the sky, was almost setting behind Kettleness; its red light was spread over East Cliff and the Abbey, making everything seem bathed in a beautiful pink glow.</t2112> <t2113>We stood in silence for a while, and suddenly Lucy murmured, as if talking to herself:</t2113>
<t2114>“Those red eyes again!”</t2114> <t2115>“Just like those eyes.”</t2115> <t2116>It was such a strange, unrelated remark that it quite frightened me.</t2116> <t2117>I turned a bit to get a better look at Lucy without seeming to stare at her and saw that she was in a semi-dazed state with a strange, incomprehensible expression on her face; <t2133>He looks so sweet while sleeping; but he looks paler than usual and there is a tired and weary shadow under his eyes that I don't quite like.</t2133> <t2134>I'm afraid there is something troubling him.</t2134> <t2135>I wish I could find out what it is.</t2135>
<t2136>August 15.</t2136> <t2137>- I woke up later than usual.</t2137> <t2138>Lucy was sluggish and tired, and she continued to sleep even after we were called downstairs.</t2138> <t2139>There was a happy surprise waiting for us at breakfast.</t2139> <t2140>Arthur's father is better and wants the wedding to take place as soon as possible.</t2140> <t2141>Lucy was filled with quiet joy; her mother looked both pleased and sad at the same time.</t2141> <t2142>She told me the reason later in the day.</t2142> <t2143>She is sad because she will lose her dear Lucy, but happy that she will soon have someone to protect her.</t2143> <t2144>Poor sweet woman!</t2144> <t2145>She told me she had received her death warrant.</t2145> <t2146>She hasn't told Lucy and made me promise to keep it a secret; her doctor said that due to her weak heart, she would die within a few months at most.</t2146> <t2147>At any moment, even now, a sudden shock would certainly kill the mother.</t2147> <t2148>Oh, we were wise to keep that terrible night of Lucy's sleepwalking from her.</t2148>
<t2149>August 17.</t2149> <t2150>- I haven't written in my journal for exactly two days.</t2150> <t2151>I didn't have the heart to write.</t2151> <t2152>It's as if a dark veil has been cast over our happiness.</t2152> <t2153>No news from Jonathan, and as her mother's final hours draw near, Lucy is growing weaker.</t2153> <t2154>I can't understand why Lucy is fading away like this.</t2154> <t2155>She eats well, sleeps well, and enjoys the fresh air, but the roses in her cheeks keep fading and she grows weaker and more lethargic each day; at night, I can almost hear her struggling to breathe.</t2155> <t2156>I always keep the key to our door tied to my wrist at night, but she gets up and walks around the room and sits by the open window.</t2156> <t2157>Last night, when I woke up, I found her hanging out of the window, and when I tried to wake her, I couldn't; she was in a trance.</t2157> <t2158>When I finally managed to bring her around, she was as weak as water and silently cried between her long, painful struggles to breathe.</t2158> <t2159>When I asked her how she got to the window, she shook her head and turned away.</t2159> <t2160>I hope her feeling ill isn't because of that unfortunate pin prick.</t2160> <t2161>Now, while she was sleeping, I looked at her throat and the tiny wounds still don't seem to be healing.</t2161> <t2162>They are still open and, on the contrary, they appear to be wider and the edges slightly whitened.</t2162> <t2163>The centers are red with small white spots.</t2163> <t2164>If they don't heal in a day or two, I will insist that the doctor looks at them.</t2164>

<t2195>August 18.</t2195> <t2196>- Today I am happy and writing while sitting on a bench in the church garden.</t2196> <t2197>Lucy is much better.</t2197> <t2198>Last night, she slept well all night and did not disturb me even once.</t2198> <t2199>Although she still looks distressingly pale and exhausted, it is as if the roses have returned to her cheeks.</t2199> <t2200>If she had anemia, I could understand it, but she does not.</t2200> <t2201>Her spirits are high, full of life and joy.</t2201> <t2202>She seems to have moved away from all that frightening silence and, as if I needed reminding, she reminded me of that night and how I found her here, sleeping on this very bench.</t2202> <t2203>As she was telling me these things, she was joyfully tapping the stone slab on the ground with the heel of her boot:</t2203>
<t2204>“Then my poor little feet didn't make much noise!</t2204> <t2205>I think if poor old Mr. Swales were here, he would tell me it was because I didn't want to wake Geordie.”</t2205> <t2206>Since he was so talkative, I asked him if he had any dreams that night.</t2206> <t2207>Before answering, that sweet, wrinkled expression that Arthur - I call him Arthur too, as is Lucy's habit - said he loved came over his face; indeed, I am not surprised that Arthur says so.</t2207> <t2208>Then, as if trying to remind himself, he continued his words in a half-dazed manner:</t2208>
<t2209>“I didn't dream much; but everything felt real.</t2209> <t2210>I just wanted to be here, at this point - I don't know why, but I was afraid of something - I don't know what it was.</t2210> <t2211>I think I was sleeping, but I remember passing through the streets and over the bridge.</t2211> <t2212>As I was passing, a fish jumped, I bent down to look at it, and as I was climbing the stairs, I heard a lot of dogs barking - it was as if the whole town was full of dogs barking in unison.</t2212> <t2213>Then, vaguely, I remember something both very sweet and very bitter surrounding me, along with a long and dark figure with red eyes, just like we saw at sunset; then it felt like I was diving into deep green waters and a song was playing in my ears, just like the ones I heard in the ears of drowning people; then everything seemed to move away from me; it was as if my soul had left my body and was flying in the air.</t2213> <t2214>At one point, I remember the West Lighthouse being right below me, then a painful feeling came over me as if I was caught in an earthquake, and I came back, I saw you shaking my body.</t2214> <t2215>I saw you doing it before I felt you.</t2215><t2216>”</t2216>
<t2217>Then she started laughing.</t2217> <t2218>What she told me seemed a bit eerie and I listened to her without taking a breath.</t2218> <t2219>I didn't quite like what she was saying and thought it would be better to steer her mind away from this topic; thus, we shifted to other subjects and Lucy returned to her old self.</t2219> <t2220>When we got home, the fresh breeze invigorated her, and her once pale cheeks were now truly pinker.</t2220> <t2221>Her mother was very happy to see her and we had a very pleasant evening together.</t2221>
<t2222>August 19.</t2222> <t2223>- Joy! Joy! Joy! Even if not everything is joyful.</t2223> <t2224>Finally, there is news from Jonathan.</t2224> <t2225>My poor darling was sick; that's why he didn't write.</t2225> <t2226>Now that I know what happened, I'm not afraid to think or say it.</t2226> <t2227>Mr. Hawkins sent me the letter, and he also wrote himself, oh, how thoughtful.</t2227> <t2228>I will leave here in the morning to go to Jonathan, and if necessary, help with his treatment and bring him home.</t2228> <t2229>Mr. Hawkins says it wouldn't be a bad thing to get married there.</t2229> <t2230>When I read the kind-hearted Sister's letter, I cried so much that I am now holding it to my chest and feeling its wetness.</t2230> <t2231>The letter is from Jonathan and it should stay close to my heart because Jonathan is in my heart.</t2231> <t2232>The details of my journey have been planned and my suitcase is packed.</t2232> <t2233>I am only taking one dress besides what I am wearing; Lucy will bring my luggage to London and keep it there until I ask for it, because...</t2233> <t2234>I should not write more; I should save these for Jonathan, my husband.</t2234> <t2235>The letter he saw and touched should comfort me until I meet him.</t2235>

<t2237>St. Mary Hospital; Budapest-</t2237>
<t2238>Letter to Miss Wilhelmina Murray</t2238>
<t2239>August 12</t2239>
<t2240>Dear Madam,</t2240>
<t2241>Thanks be to God, Saint Joseph, and Mother Mary, I am writing on behalf of Mr. Jonathan Harker, whose condition is improving but who does not yet have the strength to write himself.</t2241> <t2242>He has been under our care for about six weeks, suffering from severe brain fever.</t2242> <t2243>He wishes me to convey his love and, while writing to Mr. Peter Hawkins in Exeter on his behalf, to extend his deepest respects, apologize for the delay, and inform that all his tasks are completed.</t2243> <t2244>He needs to rest for a few weeks in our sanatorium in the mountains, but he will return afterward.</t2244> <t2245>He desires me to say that he does not have enough money with him and wishes to pay for the days he has spent here so that others in need of help are not deprived.</t2245>
<t2246>With my love and blessings...</t2246>
<t2247>NUN AGATHA</t2247>
<t2248>Note: I am opening the letter while I am still asleep to let you know one more thing.</t2248> <t2249>He told me everything about you and said that you would soon be his wife.</t2249> <t2250>I extend my best wishes to both of you!</t2250> <t2251>He experienced a horrifying shock - our doctor says so -; the things he raved about while he was not himself were hair-raising; wolves, poison, and blood; ghouls and evil spirits and many other terrifying things that I fear to name.</t2251> <t2252>Always be careful to prevent such things from exciting him in the future; the traces of illnesses like his do not easily disappear.</t2252> <t2253>We should have written to you long ago, but we had no knowledge of his friends and found nothing on him that anyone could understand.</t2253> <t2254>He arrived here by train from Klausenberg, and according to what the station master told the conductor, he had run into the station and shouted that he wanted a ticket home.</t2254> <t2255>Realizing from his harsh behavior that he was English, they gave him a ticket to the farthest station reached by the train on this route.</t2255>
<t2256>Make sure she is well taken care of.</t2256> <t2257>She won all hearts with her sweetness and kindness.</t2257> <t2258>She is genuinely getting better, and I have no doubt that she will fully recover in a few weeks.</t2258> <t2259>But for your safety, keep an eye on her.</t2259> <t2260>I pray to God, Saint Joseph, and Mother Mary that you have many long and happy years ahead.</t2260>
<t2261>Dr. Seward's journal</t2261>
<t2262>August 19.</t2262> <t2263>- Last night, there was a strange and sudden change in Renfield.</t2263> <t2264>Around eight o'clock, he began to get excited and sniff around like a dog.</t2264> <t2265>The attendant, who was affected by his behavior and knew of my interest in him, encouraged Renfield to talk.</t2265> <t2266>Renfield is generally respectful to the attendant and sometimes even fawns over him, but the man said he was very arrogant tonight.</t2266> <t2267>He did not deign to speak in any manner.</t2267> <t2268>The only thing he said was:</t2268>
